Flower Light your title, bright and clear,
Immeasurable beings, you'll hold so dear.
To countless Buddhas, offerings made,
Bodhisattva practices, fully arrayed.
With ten great powers, and merits grand,
The supreme Way, you'll understand.
After kalpas, beyond all count,
Great Jewel Adornment, the time will mount.
Stainless the world, in purity it lies,
Without blemish, before all eyes.
Lapis lazuli, the ground so deep,
Golden ropes, the pathways keep.
Of seven treasures, trees brightly gleam,
Always bearing flowers, a lovely dream.
The Bodhisattvas, in that fair land,
Firm resolve and steady hand.
Supernatural powers, perfected quite,
All fully complete, a wondrous sight.
The Bodhisattva Way, superbly learned,
Under countless Buddhas, lessons earned.
Such great beings, pure and true,
By Flower Light Buddha, converted anew.
When the Buddha was a prince, so bold,
His country and honor, he let unfold.
In his very last body, free from strife,
Renounced his home, and found new life.
The Flower Light Buddha, in the world will stay,
For twelve minor kalpas, come what may.
The people of his land, with spirits bright,
Will live for eight minor kalpas, bathed in light.
After the Buddha passes, as all things do,
The True Dharma remains, steadfast and true.
For thirty-two minor kalpas, it will reside,
Widely saving beings, with naught to hide.
When the True Dharma completely fades,
The Dharma-Image lasts, through future decades.
His relics widely distributed, far and wide,
From gods and humans, offerings to abide.
What the Flower Light Buddha will decree,
Is all like this, for all to see.
He, the holy and Double-Footed Honored One,
Most supreme and equal to none.
He is you, no doubt, no fear,
Be delighted, congratulate yourself, my dear.
At that time, the four groups of the assembly—monks, nuns, laymen, and laywomen, along with the gods, dragons, yakshas, gandharvas, asuras, garudas, kinnaras, and mahoragas—seeing Śāriputra receive the prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi directly from the Buddha, were filled with great joy and unbounded delight. They each took off their upper garments to offer them to the Buddha. Śakra, the King of the Devas, the Brahma King, and others, along with countless heavenly beings, also offered wonderful heavenly robes and heavenly maṇḍārava and mahāmaṇḍārava flowers to the Buddha. The heavenly robes they scattered remained in the air and turned by themselves. The heavenly music, of hundreds of thousands of kinds, played all at once in the air, raining down all the heavenly flowers. They said: “The Buddha first turned the Dharma wheel in Benares, and today he turns the supreme and greatest Dharma wheel once more.”
At that time, the heavenly beings, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
In Benares of old, a truth did unfold,
The Dharma's great wheel, a story of old.
Of aggregates five, their rise and their fall,
The Dharma was preached, a message for all.
Now turns again, a wonder to see,
This supreme great wheel, for all to be free.
So subtle, profound, this truth we now hear,
But few can believe, and few hold it dear.
Though many a time, the Honored One spoke,
Such depth and such grace, we never awoke
To a Dharma so grand, so subtle and high,
We rejoice in its sound, as it fills up the sky.
Great Śāriputra, a prophecy won,
And we, too, shall follow, until we are one
With the Buddhas, most honored, supreme in all lands,
A Way inconceivable, held in our hands.
With skillfulest means, the Buddha does teach,
The merits we hold, within our soul's reach,
From this life and past, and the Buddha's own sight,
To the Buddha Way now, we dedicate all with our might.
At that time, Śāriputra said to the Buddha: “World-Honored One, I am no longer in doubt, as I have received the prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi directly from the Buddha. But these twelve hundred Arhats who are free in mind, in the past they were still in the stage of learning. The Buddha always taught them, saying, ‘My Dharma can free you from birth, old age, sickness, and death and lead you to ultimate nirvana.’ These learners and non-learners also thought that by freeing themselves from the view of a self and the views of existence and non-existence, they had attained nirvana. But now, in the presence of the World-Honored One, they have heard what they have never heard before and are all in doubt. Please, World-Honored One, we ask that you explain the reason for this for the four groups of the assembly, so they may be freed from their doubts and regrets.”
At that time, the Buddha said to Śāriputra: “Did I not say before that the World-Honored Buddhas use various causes, parables, and words, and expedient means to preach the Dharma, all for the sake of Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi? All these teachings were to convert Bodhisattvas. But Śāriputra, I will now use a parable to further clarify this meaning. For all wise people, a parable helps them to understand.”
The Parable of the Burning House
“Śāriputra, imagine a village or town where there is a great elder who is old and frail. He has boundless wealth, many fields, houses, and servants. His house is vast, but it has only one gate. It is home to many people—one hundred, two hundred, even five hundred—who live there. The halls and pavilions are old and decaying, the walls are collapsing, the pillars are rotten at their base, and the beams are dangerously leaning. All around, a great fire suddenly erupts, burning down the house. The elder’s children, whether ten, twenty, or thirty of them, are inside this house. The elder sees this great fire starting on all four sides and is greatly alarmed. He thinks: ‘Although I can escape through this one burning gate in safety, my children, who are inside this burning house, are attached to their games and do not feel or know the danger. They are not alarmed or frightened. The fire is coming closer to their bodies, and the pain is about to reach them, but their hearts do not feel loathing, and they have no intention of getting out.’”
“Śāriputra, the elder then thinks: ‘I am strong and capable, so I will take them out of the house in a basket or on a table.’ But then he thinks again: ‘This house has only one gate, and it is narrow and small. My children are young and ignorant. They are attached to their playthings and might fall and be burned by the fire. I must speak to them of the danger. This house is already on fire. They should quickly get out now, so they will not be burned and harmed by the fire.’ Having thought this, he told his children just as he had planned: ‘You must get out quickly!’ The father, out of compassion, used gentle words and parables to persuade them, but the children were attached to their games and would not believe him. They were not alarmed or afraid and had no intention of getting out. They also did not know what fire was, what the house was, or what a loss it would be. They just ran back and forth, playing, and only looked at their father.”
“At that time, the elder thought: ‘This house is now engulfed in a great fire. If I and my children do not get out now, we will surely be burned. I must now devise an expedient means to enable my children to escape this danger.’ The father knew that his children had various things they liked, all sorts of rare and curious toys, and that their hearts were surely attached to them. So he told them: ‘The various rare and wonderful playthings that you love are difficult to get. If you do not take them now, you will surely regret it later. All these different goat carts, deer carts, and ox carts are now outside the gate for you to play with. You should quickly come out of this burning house! Whatever you wish for, I will give it all to you.’”
“At that time, when the children heard their father speak of the precious toys, which suited their desires, their hearts were all filled with courage. They pushed and shoved each other, racing to get out of the burning house. At that moment, the elder saw that all his children had safely escaped. They were all sitting in the open, at the crossroad, with no more danger. Their minds were at ease, and they were filled with boundless joy. Then the children said to their father: ‘Father, you promised us the wonderful toys, the goat carts, the deer carts, and the ox carts. Please give them to us now.’”
“Śāriputra, what do you think? Was the elder being deceitful when he gave each of his children a single, magnificent, jeweled great cart?”
Śāriputra said: “No, World-Honored One. The elder’s only intention was to get his children out of the fire and save their lives. It was not a lie. Why? By saving their lives, they had already gained the most wonderful treasure. What is more, he used an expedient means to rescue them from the burning house. World-Honored One, even if that elder had not given them a single small cart, it would still not have been a lie. Why? The elder’s first thought was: ‘I will use an expedient means to get my children out.’ For this reason, there was no falsehood. How much more so, when the elder knew his own boundless wealth and, wishing to benefit his children, gave each of them a great cart.”
The Buddha said to Śāriputra: “Well said, well said. It is just as you have said. Śāriputra, the Tathagata is also like this. He is the father of all the world. He has completely extinguished all fear, decay, worries, and afflictions and has perfected immeasurable wisdom and insight, power, and fearlessness. He has great supernatural powers and the power of wisdom. He is complete in the perfection of expedient means and wisdom, and his great compassion is constant and tireless. He constantly seeks what is good to benefit all.
He is born into the decaying, burning house of the three realms to save sentient beings from the fire of birth, old age, sickness, and death, sorrow, grief, suffering, and affliction, ignorance, and the three poisons. He teaches and converts them so they may attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. He sees that all sentient beings are being burned by birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, grief, and suffering. They also receive various sufferings because of the five desires and the pursuit of wealth. Moreover, because of their greedy attachment and pursuit, they receive present sufferings and will later receive the sufferings of the hells, animal realms, and hungry ghosts. Or, if they are born in the heavens or among humans, they suffer from poverty, separation from loved ones, and the pain of meeting those they hate, and so on.
Sentient beings are sunk in these sufferings but are joyful and play games. They do not feel or know it; they are not alarmed or frightened. They do not feel loathing or seek liberation. They run back and forth in this burning house of the three realms. Although they encounter great suffering, they do not consider it a problem. Śāriputra, when the Buddha sees this, he thinks: ‘I am the father of sentient beings. I should pull them out of this suffering and danger and give them the joy of the Buddha’s boundless and immeasurable wisdom, so they may play happily.’”
“Śāriputra, the Tathagata also thinks: ‘If I were to abandon expedient means and use only my supernatural powers and the power of my wisdom to praise the Tathagata’s wisdom and insight, powers, and fearlessness for sentient beings, they would not be saved by this. Why? These sentient beings have not yet escaped birth, old age, sickness, death, sorrow, grief, and suffering and are being burned by the fire of the three realms. How could they comprehend the Buddha’s wisdom?’”
“Śāriputra, it is like that elder, who, although he had great physical strength, did not use it. Instead, he used earnest expedient means to rescue his children from the danger of the burning house and then gave each of them a great jeweled cart. The Tathagata is also like this. Although he has power and fearlessness, he does not use them. Instead, he uses the wisdom of expedient means to rescue sentient beings from the burning house of the three realms. He preaches the three vehicles—the śrāvaka vehicle, the pratyekabuddha vehicle, and the Buddha vehicle—and says: ‘You should not delight in dwelling in the burning house of the three realms. Do not covet the coarse and broken forms, sounds, smells, tastes, and sensations. If you are greedy and attached to them, you will be burned. You must quickly leave the three realms. You will then attain the three vehicles—the śrāvaka vehicle, the pratyekabuddha vehicle, and the Buddha vehicle. I will guarantee this for you now; it is not a lie. You should only diligently cultivate with vigor.’ The Tathagata uses this expedient means to induce and lead sentient beings. He then says: ‘You should know that these three Dharmas are all praised by the sages. They are free, unattached, and without seeking. By riding in these three vehicles, you will find joy in the unconditioned roots, powers, awakenings, ways, meditation, liberation, and samadhis, and you will attain immeasurable, peaceful, and joyful happiness.’”
“Śāriputra, if there are sentient beings who have an inner nature of wisdom, who hear the Dharma from the World-Honored Buddha, believe in it, and with earnest diligence wish to quickly leave the three realms to seek their own nirvana, this is called the śrāvaka vehicle, like those children who sought the goat cart and came out of the burning house. If there are sentient beings who hear the Dharma from the World-Honored Buddha, believe in it, and with earnest diligence seek self-enlightened wisdom, delighting in quiet solitude and knowing the causes and conditions of all Dharmas, this is called the pratyekabuddha vehicle, like those children who sought the deer cart and came out of the burning house. If there are sentient beings who hear the Dharma from the World-Honored Buddha, believe in it, and diligently cultivate with vigor, seeking all-knowing wisdom—the Buddha’s wisdom, self-attained wisdom, and the wisdom of a teacherless teacher, as well as the Tathagata’s wisdom, powers, and fearlessness—and who pity and seek to bring peace and joy to immeasurable sentient beings and to benefit gods and humans, saving them all, this is called the Great Vehicle. The Bodhisattvas seek this vehicle, and so they are called Mahāsattvas, like those children who sought the ox cart and came out of the burning house.”
“Śāriputra, just as that elder saw that all his children had safely escaped the burning house and reached a place of no fear, he thought of his boundless wealth and gave each of them a great cart. The Tathagata is also like this. He is the father of all sentient beings. When he sees countless millions of sentient beings escape the suffering of the three realms and their dangerous path through the gate of the Buddha’s teaching and attain the joy of nirvana, the Tathagata then thinks: ‘I have a treasury of immeasurable and boundless wisdom, powers, and fearlessness, and all the Dharmas of a Buddha. These sentient beings are all my children, and I will give them the Great Vehicle equally. I will not allow anyone to attain extinction alone.’ He saves all of them with the Tathagata’s extinction. Those sentient beings who have escaped the three realms are all given the tools of the Buddhas’ meditation and liberation to enjoy. These are all of one form and one kind, praised by the sages, and can create pure, wonderful, and supreme happiness.”
“Śāriputra, just as that elder first used three carts to lure his children and then gave them only one great cart, adorned with treasures, which was the most safe and supreme, that elder was not guilty of a lie. The Tathagata is also like this; he is not guilty of any lie. He first preaches the three vehicles to guide sentient beings, and then he saves and liberates them with only the Great Vehicle. Why? Because the Tathagata has an immeasurable treasury of wisdom, powers, and fearlessness—all the Dharmas—and is able to give the Great Vehicle Dharma to all sentient beings, even if they cannot all receive it completely.”
“Śāriputra, for this reason, you should know that due to the power of the Buddhas’ expedient means, they differentiate and speak of three vehicles, although there is only One Buddha Vehicle.”
The Buddha, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Imagine an elder, with a house grand and old,
Worn out and dilapidated, a story untold.
Halls and rooms dangerous, pillars crumbling low,
Beams and ridgepoles leaning, foundations let go.
Walls cracked and caved in, mud plaster now shed,
Thatch scattered and falling, rafters all dread.
A fence all around, crooked and filled with such grime,
Five hundred souls dwelling, trapped within time.
Owls, vultures, and eagles, crows, pigeons, and doves,
Vipers and snakes, with scorpions it moves.
Centipedes, millipedes, geckos that crawl,
Poisonous insects, within every wall.
Monkeys and civets, rats and small mice,
Vicious bugs running, a terrible price.

The stench of foul waste, flowing everywhere,
Dung beetles and insects, gathering there.
Foxes and wolves, and wild dogs that roam,
Gnawing and trampling, far from a home.
They bite and they chew on the bodies now dead,
Bones and flesh scattered, a horrific spread.
Packs of dogs fighting, for food they now vie,
Hungry and skinny, beneath a dark sky.
Confused and searching, for sustenance found,
They fight and they tug, with a terrible sound.
Baring their teeth, with a mournful, deep howl,
The house is quite terrifying, a dark, ghostly cowl.

Demons and spirits, and ghosts all around,
Yakshas and evil, on this cursed ground.
Eating human flesh, with poisonous sting,
Evil birds and beasts, their young they now bring.
Each one is hiding, their offspring to keep,
While yakshas are fighting, and secrets run deep.
Striving to snatch, and the young ones to eat,
When full from their feasting, their evil complete.
The sound of their fighting, a frightening call,
The Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts squat, on mounds in the hall.
Or sometimes they fly, a foot or two high,
Wandering and playing, beneath a dark sky.
They grab a dog's legs, and throw it with might,
Making it voiceless, a terrible sight.
A foot on its neck, for their wicked delight,
Frightening the dog, in the dark of the night.

Other ghosts gather, with bodies so vast,
Naked and skinny and black, their dark shadows cast.
They constantly live there, with great evil cries,
Crying and searching, beneath darkened skies.
Other ghosts wait, with throats thin as needles,
Other ghosts watch, with heads like huge bee-dles.
Some eat human flesh, or devour the dogs,
Their hair wild and tangled, through marshy, dark bogs.
Cruel and ferocious, by hunger now pressed,
They howl and they run, with no moment of rest.

The yakshas, hungry ghosts, and evil birds of the air,
In desperate hunger, they look everywhere.
Peeking in windows, and doors all around,
All these dangers and fears, on this cursed ground.
Immeasurable terrors, within this old keep,
This old ruined house, where dark secrets sleep,
Belongs to one person, whose fate is now sealed.

This man recently went out, his presence concealed,
Not long before, in the house's deep rear,
A fire suddenly started, bringing forth fear.
On all four sides at once, the flames fiercely blazed,
Beams, ridgepoles, and pillars, in a thunderous daze.
Exploded and cracked, then collapsed with a roar,
The walls crumbled and fell, to the dust on the floor.
The ghost-spirits cried out, with a terrible sound,
As eagles and vultures, flew high all around.

The Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts, in a confused, frightened state,
Could not get themselves out, sealed by cruel fate.
The evil beasts hid, and poisonous things crawled,
In holes and in cracks, as the burning house called.
The Piśāca ghosts lived there, within the dark space,
Their meager merit failing, in this burning disgrace.
Pressed by the fire, they hurt and they harmed,
Drinking blood, eating flesh, by the danger alarmed.

The wild dogs, and others, had already died,
Great evil beasts came, to feast side by side.
A foul, smoky haze, filled all sides with its blight,
Centipedes, millipedes, in the dim, fading light.
Poisonous snakes, by the fire now burned,
Raced out of their holes, as the lesson was learned.
The Kumbhāṇḍa ghosts grabbed them, and ate them with glee,
And hungry ghosts, with fire on their heads, you see,
Suffering from hunger, and thirst, and the heat,
Ran around in a daze, with their blistering feet.
The house was like this, frightening and dire,
The poisonous, harmful, disastrous fire.
The multitude of dangers, not merely one,
As the terrible saga, had only begun.

At that time, the owner, stood outside the gate,
Heard someone declare, sealing their children's sad fate:
"Your children, because of their innocent games,
Came into this house, now consumed by the flames.
They are young and quite ignorant, in play they delight,
Attached to their games, in the fading of light."

When the elder heard this, he was startled and then,
Entered the burning house, a brave, noble man.
He thought he should rescue, and save them from harm,
Lest they be burned wholly, by the fire's alarm.
He told his dear children, and gave his command,
Explaining the dangers, throughout the whole land:
"The evil ghosts, poisonous insects now fly,
And the fire is raging, beneath a hot sky!
The various sufferings, continuous and strong,
Following swiftly, for far too long.
The poisonous snakes, and the vipers that crawl,
As well as the yakshas, within this dark wall.
The Kumbhāṇā ghosts, and the wild, savage hound,
Foxes and jackals, on this burning ground.

The vultures, and owls, and the birds of the air,
And the centipedes crawling, beyond all compare.
Are starving and thirsty, in desperate plight,
And are extremely frightening, in this terrible light.
This place is so full of suffering and dread,
Not to mention the great fire, now fiercely spread!"

The children were ignorant, in their innocent play,
Though they heard their father's words, they wished to stay.
Still attached to their happiness, they would not cease,
Their games and their laughter, defying all peace.
At that time, the elder, thought to himself then,
"My children are like this, again and again,
Increasing my worry, and sadness profound.
In this house of peril, no joy can be found.
But my children are captured, by games and by fun,
And will not accept instruction, until it is done.
They will surely be harmed, by the merciless fire."

He immediately thought, with a fervent desire,
A skillful means to set his dear children free.
He told his children, with great certainty:
"I have all kinds of wonderful playthings, so grand,
Wondrous jeweled carts, now outside the land.
Goat carts, deer carts, and great ox carts you'll see,
Now outside the gate, just waiting for thee.
You must come out quickly, and I will then give,
These treasures unto you, that you may then live.

I have made these fine carts, expressly for you,
You can play with them freely, your wishes come true."
When the children heard this, of all these fine carts,
They immediately raced, with joyful young hearts.
To get out of the house, away from all fright,
They reached the open ground, in the sun's warm light.
Away from all sufferings, and dangers so great,
Escaping the burning, before it was late.

When the elder saw, his children were free,
Had gotten out safely, for all eyes to see.
And were at the crossroads, now safe from the blaze,
He sat on a lion's seat, in triumphant haze.
And congratulated himself, with joy in his soul,
Saying, "I am now joyful, and peacefully whole.
These children, who were so difficult to raise,
Were foolish and ignorant, in their childish ways.
But they entered the dangerous house, so forlorn,

With so many poisonous insects, and demons now born.
The great fire raged fiercely, all around and around,
But I have saved these children, on this hallowed ground.
And caused them to escape, the danger so dire.
For this reason, all people, I am now peaceful, desire."

At that time, the children, knew father was free,
And sitting at ease, for all eyes to see.
They all went to him, in the sun's golden gleam,
And said, "Please give us, the carts of our dream.

The three kinds of jeweled carts, so wondrous and grand,
You promised before, throughout the whole land.
'When you children come out, from the fire's hot breath,
I will give you the three carts,' avoiding all death.
'According to your wishes,' so now is the time,
Please give them to us," in their youthful prime.

The elder was wealthy, with riches untold,
His storerooms were plentiful, worth more than gold.
With gold, silver, lapis lazuli bright,
Tridacna, and agate, shining with light.
He made all the great carts, with treasures so rare,
Adorned and decorated, beyond all compare.

With railings all around, and bells softly chiming,
Hanging on all four sides, in perfect, sweet timing.
And golden ropes interwoven, a beautiful sight,
A net of fine pearls, spread above them so bright.
Golden flowers and garlands, hung down everywhere,
Many adornments, filling the air.
Surrounded the carts, with silk floss so soft,
Used as mattresses, from the ground lifted aloft.
The most wonderful cloth, fine beyond measure,
Worth thousands of millions, a priceless, rare treasure.
Fresh, white, and clean, covered each cart,
A masterpiece of beauty, a work of fine art.

There were great white oxen, fat, strong, and so bold,
With beautiful bodies, a story untold.
To pull the jeweled carts, with power and grace,
Many attendants, with smiles on each face.
Waited upon them, with these wonderful carts,
He gave them to all, with loving, true hearts.
His children receiving, in equal degree,

At that time, the children, with joy and with glee,
Were filled with delight, for all eyes to see.
They rode in these jeweled carts, with hearts light and free,
And traveled in all four directions, with pure ecstasy.
Playing so happily, without any strife,
Freely and unhindered, throughout their whole life.

He said to Śāriputra: "I am like this, my friend.
I am the Honored One, whose teachings transcend.
The father of the world, for all to embrace,
All sentient beings, my children of grace.

And they are deeply attached, to worldly delight,
Without a mind of wisdom, in shadows of night.
The three realms are not safe, just like a house ablaze,
It is full of much suffering, in countless dark ways.

And is extremely frightening, with worries so deep,
Of birth, old age, sickness, and death, while they sleep.
Such fires are burning, fiercely and without end,
The Tathagata has left, on whom we depend.
The burning house of the three realms, now far behind,

He lives in a stillness, a peace of the mind.
Resting comfortably, in the forest's soft embrace,
Now, these three realms, belong to my grace.
And the sentient beings, who dwell within them,
Are all my own children, a precious, bright gem.

But now this place, is full of great fears,
Only I, all alone, can wipe away tears.
Can save and protect them, with teachings so true,
Though I may teach them, what can I do?
They do not believe, or accept what I say,
Because they are deeply attached, day after day,
To the defilement of desires, a powerful hold,

For this reason, with expedient means, so bold,
I preach the three vehicles, a story unfolds.
To cause all sentient beings, to know of the pain,
The suffering of the three realms, again and again.
I reveal and expound, the path pure and bright,
The path that is beyond, the world's fading light.

These children, if their minds are firm and quite strong,
Will be complete in three wisdoms, where they truly belong.
And the six supernatural powers, a wondrous gift,
Some will attain pratyekabuddha status, a spiritual lift.
Or become a non-retrogressing Bodhisattva, so grand,

You, Śāriputra, I use this command,
This parable for sentient beings, throughout the whole land.
To speak of the One Buddha Vehicle, so true,
If you all can believe, and accept what I do,

You will all attain, the Buddha Way's grace.
This vehicle is subtle, and wonderful, takes its place.
Pure, and supreme, beyond all compare,

In all of the worlds, there's nothing above there.
It is praised by the Buddha, and should be adored,
Made offerings to, and revered and explored.
By all sentient beings, with hearts pure and keen,
The immeasurable thousands, of powers, I've seen.
And liberations, meditation, and wisdom's bright flame,
And the other Dharmas of the Buddha, by this holy name,
One attains this vehicle, by which all the children,

Can play, day and night, for kalpas, again and again.
Along with all the Bodhisattvas, and the śrāvaka throng,
They ride this jeweled vehicle, where they truly belong.
Straight to the place of enlightenment, so pure and so high,
For this reason, in ten directions, you must truly try,
And search, for no other vehicle, will you ever discern,

Besides the Buddha’s expedient means, a lesson to learn.
I tell Śāriputra: All of you here,
Are my children, so precious and dear.

And I am your father, who watches with care,
You have been burned by the sufferings, beyond all compare.
Of countless kalpas, for so very long,
I have rescued and pulled you, from where you belonged.

Causing you to leave, the three realms, in their plight,
Although I previously told you, with wisdom and light,
That you had attained extinction, an end to all strife,
You have only ended, the cycle of life and death.
You have not truly, become extinct, my dear friend.

What you should now do, until the very end,
Is to attain only, the wisdom of the Buddha, so grand.
If there is a Bodhisattva, throughout this whole land,
In this assembly, with pure heart and mind,

Who can listen with oneness, to truth you will find.
To the true Dharma of the Buddhas, so wise and so deep,
The World-Honored Buddhas, their promises keep.
Although they use expedient means, for all eyes to see,

The sentient beings they convert, all Bodhisattvas will be.
If a person has little wisdom, and their mind is not clear,
And is deeply attached, to love and to fear.

For these people, I preach, the truth of all suffering,
When sentient beings hear this, their hearts start to sing.
And they gain something, they've never yet had,
The Buddha preaches the truth of suffering, both good and bad.
And it is true, and not different, for all to then learn.

If there are sentient beings, who do not discern,
The root of all suffering, where troubles reside,
And are deeply attached, to the cause deep inside.
And cannot leave it, for even a moment's sweet peace,

For these people, I use expedient means, to bring sweet release.
I preach the path to them, so they may then see,
The cause of all suffering, for you and for me.
Is greed and desire, as the root, deep and strong.

If greed and desire, are extinguished, where they don't belong,
There is nothing to rely on, as we journey along.
The complete extinction of all sufferings, a truth to embrace,
Is named the third truth, with beauty and grace.

To attain the truth of extinction, so wondrous and pure,
One must practice the Way, to forever endure.
To be free from the bonds, of all suffering's might,
Is named liberation, a glorious light.

From what are these people, now truly set free?
They are only freed from falsehood, for all eyes to see.
Which is named liberation, a freedom they gain.

But they have not truly, attained total reign.
Attained total liberation, so wondrous and grand,
The Buddha says these people, throughout the whole land,
Have not truly attained extinction, a state pure and bright,

As these people have not attained, the supreme Way's light.
My intent is not, to allow them to stop,
At extinction's sweet promise, from bottom to top.

I am the King of Dharma, at ease in its flow,
I appear in the world, so all sentient beings will know.
To bring peace and comfort, to all hearts and minds,

You, Śāriputra, this Dharma Seal one finds,
I preach it for the benefit, of the world's many kinds.

Wherever you go, you must not, you will see,
Preach it to the unworthy, for it is not for thee.
If there is someone who hears it, with joy in their heart,
Rejoices in it, and receives it from the start,
On their head, as a blessing, so pure and so deep,

You should know that this person, their promise will keep.
Is a non-retrogressing Bodhisattva, so grand,
If there is a person who believes, throughout the whole land,
And receives the true Dharma, of this holy sutra, so bright,

This person has already, seen Buddhas of light.
Seen past Buddhas, with offerings so true,
Made respectful offerings, for me and for you.
And has also heard this Dharma, a wisdom so vast.

If a person is able to believe, and their faith will then last,
In what you say, it is as if, they have seen me, so grand,
And have also seen you, throughout the whole land.

And the assembly of monks, with their wisdom and grace,
And all the Bodhisattvas, in this sacred place.
This Lotus Sutra, so profound and so deep,
Is for those with deep wisdom, whose secrets they keep.

The shallow-minded who hear it, will be confused, you will find,
And will not understand it, with an untrained mind.
The power of all the śrāvakas, so earnest and true,
And pratyekabuddhas, for me and for you,

Is insufficient for this sutra, its meaning to glean.
You, Śāriputra, could only have been,
Could only enter this sutra, through faith, strong and bright,

How much more so for the other śrāvakas, in fading light?
The other śrāvakas, because they believe in the word,
The Buddha’s words, a message they've heard.
Follow this sutra, with hearts pure and keen.

It is not a matter, of their own wisdom, it's been,
Furthermore, Śāriputra, those who are proud and lazy,
And hold the view of a self, their minds often hazy,

You must not preach this sutra, to them, you will find.
The shallow-minded person, with an ordinary mind.
Who is deeply attached, to the five desires' sweet call,
If they hear but cannot understand, they will stumble and fall,

You also should not preach it, to them, my dear friend.
If a person does not believe, and their heart will then bend,
And slanders this sutra, with words sharp and keen,
They will cut off all the Buddha seeds, that ever have been,
In the world, so wondrous.

Or if they frown, with a look so unkind,
And harbor doubts, within their own mind.
You should listen, as I describe, in tones solemn and deep,
The karmic retribution, that they then will reap.

Whether the Buddha is in the world, in light or in shade,
Or after he has passed away, his presence will fade.
If a person slanders, these kinds of true sutras, so grand,

And sees someone who reads, recites, throughout the whole land,
And upholds the sutra, with love and with grace,
They despise them, hold grudges, with hatred on their face.

And are full of jealousy, and hatred, so strong,
The karmic retribution for this person, where they truly belong,
You should listen to it now, as the story unfolds.
When this person dies, to the Avīci hell they're sold.

For a full kalpa, in torment so deep,
When the kalpa ends, they will awaken from sleep.
They will be reborn again, in this sorrowful way,
And in this way, they will revolve, for countless a day.

For countless kalpas, a cycle of pain,
When they get out of hell, they will fall once again.
Into the animal realm, a fate harsh and grim.
They will be a dog, or a wild dog, their prospects so dim.
Their shadows will be thin, and weak, you will see,

They will be dark, scabby, and leprous, for all eyes to be.
And they will be tormented, by people's harsh touch,
Furthermore, they will be despised, suffering so much.
And looked down upon by people, with scorn and with sneers.

They will always be troubled, by hunger and fears.
Their bones and their flesh, will be dry and so withered,
In life they will endure agony, by fate they are tethered.
And in death, they will be hit, with stones and with tiles,

Because they cut off the Buddha seed, for countless long miles.
They receive this karmic retribution, so harsh and so strong.
They may become a camel, where they don't belong,
Or be born as a donkey, with burdens to bear,

Their bodies always carrying, heavy loads in the air.
And being beaten with sticks, and with whips, sharp and keen,
They will only think of water, and grass, as I've seen.
And will not know anything else, in their sorrowful plight.

Because they slandered this sutra, in darkness of night,
They receive such a karmic retribution, a terrible sight.
Some may become a wild dog, with a scabby, sad face,
And enter a village, in a desolate place.

Their bodies are scabby and leprous, and in pain they will writhe,
And they have only one eye, as their fortunes then thrive.
They are hit and are thrown, by children at play,
And endure all kinds of suffering, throughout the whole day.

Sometimes even dying from it, in their sorrowful state,
When they die from this, they are reborn by fate.
In the body of a great snake, whose form is so vast,
Whose form is five hundred yojanas long, built to last.

They are deaf, foolish, and have no feet, as they go,
Rolling and slithering, on their belly, so slow.
They are gnawed and are eaten, by small insects so many,
They endure suffering, day and night, for not a penny.

Without any rest, in their torment so deep,
Because they slandered this sutra, their sorrow they keep.
They receive such a karmic retribution, a terrible cost.

If they are able to be reborn, as a human, then lost,
Their faculties are dull and ignorant, so far from the light.
They are short, ugly, crippled, lame, a sorrowful sight,
Blind, deaf, and hunchbacked, for all eyes to see.

Whatever they say, people will not agree,
People do not believe, or accept, you will find.
Their breath is always foul, leaving reason behind,
And they are possessed, by ghosts and by demons.

They are poor and of low status, with terrible portents,
And are made to work for others, with little to gain.
They are often sick and emaciated, enduring great pain,
With no one to rely on, in their sorrowful plight.

Even if they get close to people, they vanish from sight,
People do not care about them, in darkness and light.
If they get anything, they immediately lose,
A life full of sorrow, with nothing to choose.

If they practice medicine, with knowledge so vast,
And treat a disease, according to a prescription's cast.
They will only worsen, the other person's ill plight,
Or even cause their death, in the fading of light.

If they themselves are sick, with aches and with pains,
No one will save and treat them, in sun or in rains.
Even if they take good medicine, it will only get worse,
A terrible fate, a sorrowful curse.

If others rebel, with anger and might,
Plunder, rob, or steal, in the darkness of night.
These kinds of sins, will be blamed on them, it's true,
Unjustly blamed on them, for me and for you.

Such a sinner, will never then see,
A Buddha, the King of all sages, for eternity.
Who preaches the Dharma, and converts all beings,
Such a sinner, will always be born, with their sorrowful pleadings.

In difficult places, where darkness takes hold.
They will be insane and deaf, their minds will be cold,
Their minds will be confused, and they'll never then hear,
The Dharma's sweet message, for countless a year.

For countless kalpas, like the sands of the Ganges, they'll turn,
Every time they are born, they will deaf and mute learn.
Their faculties will be incomplete, a sorrowful plea,
And they will always live, in the hells, you will see,
As if they were in a garden, with flowers so bright,

In other evil destinies, in the fading of light,
It will be like their own home, where darkness takes flight.
Camels, donkeys, pigs, and dogs, throughout the whole land,
These are the places, where they then will stand.
Because they slandered this sutra, so cruel and so deep,

They receive such a karmic retribution, their sorrow they keep.
If they are reborn as a human, with faculties so frail,
They will be deaf, blind, and mute, a sorrowful tale.
And adorned with all kinds of poverty, so harsh and so grim,
And decay, in their bodies, from limb to each limb.

They will be bloated with dropsy, and wasted away,
With scabs, leprosy, boils, and sores, day after day.
Such illnesses, will be like their own clothes,
Their body will always be foul and rotten, as everyone knows.

Unclean with filth and with dirt, a terrible sight,
They will be deeply attached, to the view of a self, with all might.
And will increase their anger, like a raging wild fire,
Their lust will be burning, with fervent desire.
And they will not differentiate, between birds and beasts,
Because they slandered this sutra, their suffering now feasts.
They receive such a karmic retribution, a terrible cost.

I tell Śāriputra: If one were to speak,
Of the sins of those, who slander, throughout the whole week.
This sutra, its message, so pure and so deep,
It would not be exhausted, in a whole kalpa's sleep.

For this reason, I tell you, my friend, you will find,
Do not preach this sutra, among ignorant kind.

If there is a person, with faculties so keen,
Whose wisdom is clear, a bright and pure scene.
Who has heard much, and has a strong memory's hold,
And who seeks the Buddha Way, with a spirit so bold.

To such a person, you may preach it, with grace,
If a person has already seen, in time and in space,
Millions and hundreds of thousands of Buddhas, so grand,

And has planted roots of goodness, throughout the whole land.
With a deeply firm mind, and a heart pure and true,
To such a person, you may preach it, for me and for you.

If a person is diligent, and constantly strives,
And cultivates a mind of compassion, where true love then thrives.
And does not begrudge their life, for all eyes to see,
You may preach it, to such a person, so lovingly.

If a person is respectful, and has no other thought,
Is far from the foolish, and common, by whom they are caught.
And lives alone, in the mountains and swamps, so serene,

To such a person, you may preach it, a wondrous, bright scene.
Furthermore, Śāriputra, if you see someone now,

Who has left evil companions, and made a new vow.
And is close to good friends, with a heart pure and true,
To such a person, you may preach it, for me and for you.

If you see a son of the Buddha, with spirit so bright,
Who upholds the precepts purely, in pure, shining light.
Like a pure and bright pearl, a treasure so rare,
And seeks the Great Vehicle sutras, beyond all compare.

To such a person, you may preach it, with wisdom and grace,
If a person has no anger, with a smile on their face.
Is simple, honest, and gentle, throughout the whole day,

And always has compassion for all, in a kind, loving way.
And is respectful to all Buddhas, with reverence deep,
To such a person, you may preach it, your promises keep.

And if there is a son of the Buddha, in a great, holy throng,
With a pure heart, and with various causes, where they truly belong,

Parables, and words, that flow like a stream,
He preaches the Dharma without hindrance, a beautiful dream.
To such a person, you may preach it, with wisdom and might.

If there is a monk, who seeks all-knowing light,
Who seeks all-knowing wisdom, so wondrous and grand,
And searches for the Dharma, throughout the whole land.
In all four directions, receiving it with joined palms, so high,
On their head, a blessing, beneath a clear sky.

And who only delights in receiving, and upholding the truth,
The Great Vehicle scriptures, from childhood to youth.
And will not even accept, one verse, as they've heard,
From other sutras, with their spoken word.

To such a person, you may preach it, with heart pure and bold.
It is like a person who sincerely, and truly, has told,
Seeks the Buddha's relics, a treasure so vast.

In the same way, this person seeks the sutra, built to last.
And when they get it, they receive it, on their head, so bright,
This person will no longer seek, other sutras, in fading light.

Nor will they ever think of, with a heart pure and free,
The classics of outside paths, for all eyes to see.
To such a person, you may preach it, with wisdom and grace.

I tell Śāriputra: I have preached, in this sacred place,
These characteristics, with wisdom so vast.
Those who seek the Buddha Way, their journey will last,
Will not be exhausted, even after a kalpa, so grand.

Such people, will be able to believe, throughout the whole land,
And understand it, with wisdom's bright gleam.
You must preach, this Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma, a dream,
To them, with devotion, so pure and so deep.




Chapter 4: Belief and Understanding

At that time, the wise Subhūti, Mahākātyāyana, Mahākāśyapa, and Mahāmaudgalyāyana, having heard the unprecedented Dharma from the Buddha and seen the World-Honored One give Śāriputra the prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi, were filled with a rare and joyful feeling in their hearts. They immediately rose from their seats, straightened their robes, bared their right shoulders, knelt on their right knees, joined their palms with a single mind, bowed respectfully, and looked up at the Buddha’s revered face. They said to the Buddha:
“We are the leaders of the monastic community, and our years have passed and our bodies are decaying. We considered ourselves to have attained nirvana and to be no longer capable of anything or needing to seek Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi further. In the past, when the World-Honored One preached the Dharma for a long time, we were in the assembly, but our bodies were tired and lazy. We only thought of emptiness, signlessness, and purposelessness. We did not delight in the Bodhisattva’s Dharmas, their playful supernatural powers, purifying Buddha lands, or saving sentient beings. Why? The World-Honored One caused us to leave the three realms and attain the realization of nirvana. And now that our years have passed and our bodies are decaying, we did not give rise to a single thought of delight in the Buddha’s teaching on the Bodhisattva’s Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi.
Now, in the presence of the Buddha, we hear the śrāvakas being given a prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. Our hearts are filled with great joy and have gained what we never had. We did not think that today, we would suddenly hear this rare Dharma, and we deeply congratulate ourselves. We have gained a great benefit and immeasurable treasures that we did not even seek.”
The Parable of the Poor Son (Prose)
“World-Honored One, we now wish to speak with a parable to make this meaning clear. It is like a person who, when he was young, was foolish and left his father and ran away. He lived in another country for a long time—for ten, twenty, or even fifty years. When he had grown up, he became poor and wandered in all four directions, seeking clothing and food. He gradually traveled and eventually happened to come to his home country.
His father had been searching for his son but could not find him and had stopped in a certain city. His family was very rich with boundless treasures—gold, silver, lapis lazuli, coral, amber, crystal, pearls, and so on. His storerooms were all overflowing. He had many servants, ministers, officials, and citizens, and countless elephants, horses, carriages, and cattle. The profits from his businesses spread throughout other countries, and he had many merchants and traders.
At that time, the poor son traveled through the villages and passed through the various countries and cities, eventually reaching the city where his father had settled. The father had been thinking of his son and felt heartbroken, as they had been separated for more than fifty years. He had never spoken of this matter to anyone, only contemplating it himself with sorrow and regret. He thought to himself that he was old and decaying, had much wealth, gold, silver, and precious treasures filling his storerooms, but had no child. If he were to die suddenly, his wealth would be scattered, and he would have no one to entrust it to. For this reason, he earnestly and constantly remembered his son. He also thought: ‘If I could find my son and entrust my wealth to him, I would be at ease and happy, without any more worries.’”
“World-Honored One, at that time, the poor son, working as a day laborer, eventually arrived at his father's house and stood outside the gate. From a distance, he saw his father sitting on a lion’s seat, with a jeweled table supporting his feet. All the Brahmins, Kshatriyas, and elders respectfully surrounded him. His body was adorned with pearl necklaces, worth tens of millions, and his officials, citizens, and servants held white whisks and waited on him on his right and left. He was covered with a jeweled canopy, with banners of various flowers hanging down. Perfumed water was sprinkled on the ground, and famous flowers were scattered. Precious treasures were arranged in rows, being brought in and out. There were all kinds of such adornments, and his majesty was exceptionally revered.
When the poor son saw his father’s great power and influence, he was filled with fear and regretted coming there. He secretly thought: ‘This must be a king or someone of equal rank. This is not a place where I can earn money with my labor. It is better to go to a poor village, where I can use my strength to work and easily get food and clothing. If I stay here for a long time, I might be forced and compelled to work for him.’ Having thought this, he quickly ran away.”
“At that time, the rich elder, from his lion’s seat, saw his son and immediately recognized him. His heart was filled with great joy. He thought: ‘My wealth and treasury now have someone to entrust to! I have always thought of this son, but have had no way to see him, and now he has come on his own accord. This is a fulfillment of my wish. Although I am old and decaying, I am still greedy for my son.’ He immediately sent a nearby servant to quickly chase him and bring him back.
At that time, the servant ran quickly and grabbed him. The poor son was shocked and cried out in distress: ‘I have not wronged you! Why are you grabbing me?’ The servant held him even tighter and forcibly dragged him back. At that time, the poor son thought to himself that he was innocent, but was being imprisoned. He was sure he would die. He became even more terrified, fainted, and fell to the ground. From a distance, the father saw this and said to the servant: ‘You don’t need this person. Do not bring him back by force. Sprinkle cold water on his face to revive him, and do not speak to him further.’
Why? Because the father knew that his son’s aspirations were lowly. He knew that his own status as a rich nobleman would be a burden for his son. He knew this was his son, but he used an expedient means and did not tell anyone that this was his son. The servant said to him: ‘I will now let you go. You can go wherever you want.’ The poor son was overjoyed, gaining something he had never had before. He rose from the ground and went to the poor village to seek clothing and food.”
“At that time, the elder wished to lure his son and devised an expedient means. He secretly sent two servants, who were haggard in appearance and without dignity. He told them: ‘You can go to him and slowly tell the poor son that there is a job here and that you will give him double the usual wage. If the poor son agrees, bring him back to work. If he asks what kind of work it is, you can tell him that you will hire him to remove dung, and that the two of you will work with him.’
The two servants then went and found the poor son. When they had found him, they told him all the above. At that time, the poor son first took his wages and then proceeded to remove the dung. The father saw his son and felt pity and a strange joy. On another day, from a window, he saw his son’s body was thin and haggard, covered in dirt and dust, and was filthy and impure. He then took off his pearl necklaces, fine and soft robes, and adornments. He changed into a coarse, dirty, and grimy robe, and covered his body with dust. In his right hand, he held a tool for removing dung, pretending to be afraid of something. He said to the workers: ‘You must all work hard and not be lazy or rest.’ By using this expedient means, he got close to his son.
Later, he said to him: ‘Hey, my boy! You must always work here and not go anywhere else. I will raise your wages. Whatever you need—bowls, pots, rice, flour, salt, vinegar, and other things—do not hesitate. There will also be older servants to help you. Be at ease, I am like your father; do not worry. Why? Because I am old, and you are young and strong. When you work, you are never deceitful or lazy, nor do you show anger or complain. I have not seen any of these faults in you, unlike in other workers. From now on, I will treat you as my own son.’
At that time, the elder even gave him a new name, calling him ‘son.’ At that time, the poor son, though happy with this new life, still thought of himself as a lowly hired hand. For this reason, for twenty years, he was constantly given the job of removing dung. After this time, they had mutual trust, and he could come and go freely. However, he still lived in his original place.”
“World-Honored One, at that time, the elder became sick and knew he would not live much longer. He said to the poor son: ‘I now have a lot of gold, silver, and precious treasures, and my storerooms are overflowing. You know the exact amounts that have been brought in and out. This is what is in my heart, and you should understand my intention. Why? From now on, I am no different from you. You must put your heart into this and not allow anything to be lost.’
At that time, the poor son accepted the instructions and took charge of all the gold, silver, precious treasures, and storerooms, but he did not have a single thought of taking a meal for himself. However, he still lived in his original place and had not yet been able to abandon his lowly mindset.
After a short time, the father knew that his son’s mind had gradually opened up and that he had achieved great resolve, no longer holding his former low opinion of himself. When he was about to pass away, he summoned his son and also gathered his relatives, clan members, kings, ministers, Kshatriyas, and elders. When they had all gathered, he publicly declared: ‘Gentlemen, you should know that this is my son, whom I fathered. He left me and ran away from a certain city and wandered and suffered for more than fifty years. His original name was such-and-such, and my name is such-and-such. In the past, in my home city, I worriedly searched for him, and I suddenly met him here. This is truly my son, and I am truly his father. All my wealth and possessions are now his. He knows all that has been brought in and out before.’
World-Honored One, when the poor son heard his father’s words, he was filled with great joy, gaining what he had never had before. He thought: ‘I originally had no mind to seek anything, but now this treasure has come to me naturally.’”
“World-Honored One, the great wealthy elder is the Tathagata. We are all like the sons of the Buddha. The Tathagata always says that we are his children. World-Honored One, we have been tormented by the three sufferings in the cycle of life and death, deluded and ignorant, attached to the small Dharma. Today, the World-Honored One has caused us to contemplate and cast away the dung of all the Dharmas of empty discourse. In this, we have cultivated with diligence and attained the wages of a single day of nirvana. Having gained this, our hearts were filled with great joy and felt content. We thought to ourselves: ‘Because we have diligently cultivated the Buddha’s Dharma, our gains are great and plentiful.’
But the World-Honored One knew that our hearts were attached to meager desires and delighted in the small Dharma, so he let us go and did not differentiate for us that we would also have a share in the treasury of the Tathagata’s wisdom and insight. The World-Honored One, with the power of expedient means, preached the Tathagata’s wisdom. We received a single day’s wages of nirvana from the Buddha and thought it was a great gain. For this Great Vehicle, we had no resolve to seek it. And we, using the Tathagata’s wisdom, have revealed and expounded it for the Bodhisattvas, but for ourselves, we had no such aspirations. Why? Because the Buddha knew our hearts delighted in the small Dharma, and with the power of expedient means, he preached for us according to our needs, but we did not know that we were truly sons of the Buddha.
Now we finally know that the World-Honored One is not stingy with the Buddha’s wisdom. Why? We have been true sons of the Buddha since the past, but we only delighted in the small Dharma. If we had the mind to delight in the great, the Buddha would have preached the Great Vehicle Dharma to us. In this sutra, only the One Vehicle is preached, but in the past, in front of the Bodhisattvas, he disparaged the śrāvakas who delighted in the small Dharma. But the Buddha truly converts us with the Great Vehicle. This is why we say we originally had no mind to seek it. But now, the great treasure of the Dharma King has come to us naturally, and we have attained everything that the sons of the Buddha should attain.”
Mahākāśyapa's Verse
At that time, Mahākāśyapa, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:

Well we heard the Buddha preaching, heard him singing out today, 
Yeah we heard the Buddha preaching, singing truth along the way, 
Got a joy down deep inside us, like we never felt before this day.
The Buddha said us wanderers, said we gonna reach that shore, 
Buddha said us poor old wanderers, we gonna reach that Buddha shore, 
This treasure we ain't even asked for, coming through our daddy's door.

There was a young and foolish boy, left his daddy's house one day, 
Yeah a young and foolish boy, he just up and ran away, 
Wandered through them distant countries for fifty years of pain and gray.
His daddy kept on searching, looking everywhere around, 
That daddy kept on searching, checking every town and ground, 
Got tired of all that looking, so he settled himself down.
Built himself a mansion, had gold and silver piled high, 
Built himself a mighty mansion, treasures reaching to the sky, 
Had elephants and servants, had profits multiplying by and by.

That poor boy kept on drifting, looking for his daily bread, 
Yeah that poor boy kept on drifting, hungry and his body dead, 
Covered up in sores and scabbing, didn't know where he was being led.
One day he came wandering, wandering to his daddy's town, 
Yeah he came a-wandering, to where his daddy'd settled down, 
Saw that mansion shining golden, thought "This ain't no place for me around."
Saw his daddy on a throne, looking like a king up there, 
Saw his daddy high up on that throne, jewels hanging everywhere, 
Got scared and started running, said "I got no business here, I swear."

But daddy saw him from that window, recognized his long-lost son, 
Yeah daddy saw him through that window, knew that boy was the one, 
Sent his servants out to catch him, but that poor boy turned to run.
Boy fell down in terror, thought he gonna surely die, 
That boy fell down in pure terror, too scared to even cry, 
Daddy said "Let him go now, don't you make that poor boy lie."

So daddy got him a plan, gonna take it nice and slow, 
Yeah daddy worked out him a plan, gonna let that love just grow, 
Sent some poor-looking messengers, said "We got some work, you know."
Told him "We'll pay double wages, if you'll come and clean our place," 
"Yeah we'll pay you double wages, just come work at this pace," 
Poor boy took that dung-removing job, thought he'd found his proper space.
Daddy put on ragged clothing, got down in the dirt and grime, 
Yeah daddy shed those royal garments, working right there all the time, 
Told his boy "You keep on working, I'll treat you right, son of mine."

Twenty years of cleaning outhouses, twenty years of feeling low, 
Yeah twenty years of shoveling, moving steady, moving slow, 
Till daddy knew his boy was ready for the truth that he should know.
Daddy called him to his deathbed, called the whole town to hear, 
Called his boy to that deathbed, made the message crystal clear, 
Said "This here's my long-lost son, and everything he'll inherit here."

Poor boy heard his daddy speaking, couldn't believe what he just heard, 
Yeah that poor boy heard his daddy, every single blessed word, 
Said "I never asked for nothing, but this treasure ain't deferred."

Well the Buddha's just like that rich man, and we're the children he raised up, 
Yeah the Buddha's like that father, and we're drinking from his cup, 
We been cleaning out the outhouses, but now we're filling up.
We thought we'd reached the end, thought nirvana was enough, 
Yeah we thought we'd reached the finish, that one day's wage and stuff, 
But Buddha knew we could go higher, though the climbing might be rough.
He let us work the small jobs, didn't push us toward the throne, 
Let us work them small jobs, cleaning up and all alone, 
Waited till our hearts were ready to claim what we should've known.

Now we finally understand it, we been Buddha's sons all along, 
Yeah we finally got the message, been his children all along, 
Just been too small-minded to hear that great vehicle song.
The Buddha ain't no miser, with his wisdom he won't hold back, 
No the Buddha ain't no miser, ain't nothing that he lacks, 
We just loved them small teachings, walking on that narrow track.

But now the truth is shining, like a treasure in our hands, 
Yeah now that truth is shining, we can finally understand, 
We're the true sons of the Buddha, heirs to all his golden lands.
How we gonna thank him? Can't be done in endless time, 
How we gonna thank the Buddha? Can't repay in rhythm or rhyme, 
Even if we carried him forever, still couldn't match that grace sublime.
Buddha's got that freedom, knows what every soul needs most, 
Yeah Buddha's got that wisdom, from coast to shining coast, 
Preaches what we each can handle, like a loving, patient host.

So we singing hallelujah, got that dharma in our bones, 
We singing hallelujah, ain't wandering alone, 
We're the Buddha's own true children, coming home to claim our throne.
The blues rolls on, the dharma flows, 
From the highest peaks to the lowest lows, 
One vehicle, one path, one way it goes.

The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma, Volume Three
Translated by Tripitaka Master Kumarajiva of the Kucha Kingdom in the Later Qin Dynasty, by imperial decree.



Chapter 5: A Parable of the Medicinal Herbs

At that time, the World-Honored One said to Mahākāśyapa and the other great disciples: “Well said, well said, Kāśyapa! You have spoken well of the Tathagata’s true merits. It is truly as you say. The Tathagata also has immeasurable, boundless, and inconceivable merits. If you were to speak of them for immeasurable millions of kalpas, you could not exhaust them.
Kāśyapa, you should know that the Tathagata is the King of all Dharmas. Whatever he says is not false. He expounds the Dharma with the wisdom of expedient means for all phenomena, and the Dharma he preaches all reaches the ground of All-Knowing Wisdom. The Tathagata knows and perceives the destination of all phenomena and also knows the deeply held thoughts of all sentient beings. He has a clear and unhindered understanding, and for all phenomena, he has fully and clearly comprehended them, revealing all wisdom to sentient beings.”
The Parable of the Great Cloud
“Kāśyapa, imagine the great world of three thousand realms, with its mountains, rivers, valleys, and lands. The trees, shrubs, forests, and all the medicinal herbs born there are of different kinds, with various names and forms. A dense cloud spreads out, covering the entire great world of three thousand realms. At the same time, it pours rain equally, and its moisture is spread everywhere. The trees, shrubs, forests, and all the medicinal herbs—those with small roots, small stems, small branches, and small leaves; those with medium roots, medium stems, medium branches, and medium leaves; and those with great roots, great stems, great branches, and great leaves—all the plants, both great and small, receive the rain according to their superior, medium, or inferior capacity. The one cloud pours rain, and according to their kind and nature, they all grow, and their flowers and fruits bloom and mature. Although they are born from the same earth and nourished by the same rain, all the plants have differences.”
“Kāśyapa, you should know that the Tathagata is also like this. He appears in the world like a great cloud rising. With a great sound, he reaches universally throughout the worlds of gods, humans, and asuras, just as that great cloud covers the three thousand great worlds. In the great assembly, he proclaims: ‘I am the Tathagata, the Worthy One, the Truly and Universally Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Supreme Being, Tamer of Men, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. I save those who are unsaved, I enlighten those who are unenlightened, I bring peace to those who are not at peace, and I enable those who have not attained nirvana to attain it. I truly know this world and the world to come. I am the All-Knower, the All-Seer, the one who knows the Way, who opens the Way, and who preaches the Way. All of you—gods, humans, and asuras—should all come here to listen to the Dharma.’”
“At that time, countless millions of sentient beings came to the Buddha to listen to the Dharma. The Tathagata then observed the sharpness or dullness of the faculties of these sentient beings, their diligence or laziness. According to their capacity, he preached the Dharma to them, in countless ways, causing them all to rejoice and gain great benefit. When these sentient beings hear this Dharma, they find peace and comfort in their present lives, and they will be born in good places in their future lives. They will receive the joy of the Way and will also hear the Dharma. Having heard the Dharma, they are freed from all obstacles. For all phenomena, according to their capacity, they gradually enter the Way. It is like that great cloud that rains upon all the trees, shrubs, forests, and all the medicinal herbs, and according to their kind and nature, they are all fully nourished, and each one is able to grow.”
“The Dharma preached by the Tathagata has one form and one taste, which is the form of liberation, the form of leaving, and the form of extinction, ultimately leading to All-Knowing Wisdom. When a sentient being hears the Dharma of the Tathagata, whether they uphold it, read it, recite it, or practice it as it is taught, they do not realize the merits they gain. Why? Only the Tathagata knows the kind, form, and nature of these sentient beings. He knows what they think, what they contemplate, what they cultivate. He knows how they think, how they contemplate, how they cultivate, and with what Dharma they think, with what Dharma they contemplate, with what Dharma they cultivate, and with what Dharma they attain what Dharma. Sentient beings dwell on various grounds, but only the Tathagata sees them as they truly are, clearly and without hindrance. It is like the trees, shrubs, forests, and all the medicinal herbs, who do not know their superior, medium, or inferior nature.
The Tathagata knows the Dharma of one form and one taste, which is the form of liberation, the form of leaving, and the form of extinction, ultimately nirvana, the form of constant stillness and extinction, which ultimately returns to emptiness. When the Buddha knows this, he observes the desires of sentient beings’ hearts and protects them. Therefore, he does not immediately preach All-Knowing Wisdom to them. Kāśyapa, you are truly rare, to be able to know that the Tathagata preaches the Dharma according to what is appropriate, and to be able to believe in and accept it. Why? The World-Honored Buddhas preach the Dharma according to what is appropriate, and it is difficult to comprehend and difficult to know.”
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
The King of Dharma breaks through the chains,
 Appears in this world with healing rains,
 According to what each heart desires,
 He preaches truth that lifts us higher.
The Tathagata, oh so revered,
 His wisdom deep, his vision clear,
 He held his peace for oh so long,
 Now breaks the silence with this song.
If you got wisdom, you'll understand,
 If you're in doubt, you'll lose your stand,
 So Kāśyapa, preach what they need,
 Plant the Dharma like a seed.

Oh listen now, brothers and sisters...
Like a great cloud rising high,
 Covering earth and covering sky,
 That wisdom cloud is full of grace,
 Lightning flashing in its face.
Thunder rolling, shaking ground,
 Making everybody gather 'round,
 The sun hides low, the earth gets cool,
 Dense clouds hanging like a jewel.
Rain comes equal, rain comes down,
 Falls on country, falls on town,
 Pouring endless, pouring free,
 Saturating all you see.

Mountains, rivers, valleys deep,
 Places where the shadows sleep,
 Trees and shrubs and healing herbs,
 Great and small, they drink the words.
Seeds of grain and sugarcane,
 Grapes receiving heaven's rain,
 All get nourished, none left dry,
 All made rich from cloudy sky.
Dry land drinking, roots run deep,
 Plants and trees no longer weep,
 That cloud releases one sweet taste,
 But each one grows at its own pace.

Yes Lord, each one grows...
All the trees so tall and strong,
 Superior, medium all along,
 According to their frame and size,
 Each one drinking from the skies.
Roots and stems and branches too,
 Leaves and flowers in every hue,
 Fruits and colors shining bright,
 All made fresh by one rain's might.
According to what they can hold,
 Some get silver, some get gold,
 Nourishment is all the same,
 But each one grows in its own name.

The Buddha's just like that great cloud,
 Appears among the human crowd,
 Covering everything the same,
 Speaking truth in every name.
The great and holy World-Honored One,
 Among the gods when day is done,
 Among all beings gathered near,
 Proclaims these words for all to hear:

"I am the Tathagata, hear me now..."
"I am the Tathagata true,
 Honored One come here for you,
 I appear just like a cloud,
 To rain on every thirsty crowd.
To saturate the withered soul,
 To make the broken spirit whole,
 I cause them all to leave their pain,
 To find peace and joy again.
Peace on earth and joy above,
 The bliss of nirvana's love,
 All you gods and human friends,
 Listen with your heart and send—
Send your mind to understand,
 Come and see with open hand,
 I'm the World-Honored supreme,
 None can match this holy dream.
To bring comfort and peace to all,
 I appear when spirits fall,
 I preach the pure sweet Dharma rain,
 For all who gather here in pain."

This Dharma got one taste, one sound...
This Dharma got a single taste,
 Liberation, none to waste,
 Nirvana singing clear and free,
 One wonderful voice, one melody.
It's always cause for the Great Way,
 The Vehicle that saves the day,
 I see all beings eye to eye,
 No favorites beneath this sky.
No one closer, no one far,
 Everyone my shining star,
 No love, no hate within my soul,
 No greed to make me lose control.
I preach the Dharma to them all,
 Same for many, same for small,
 What I do for just one man,
 I do for multitudes the same plan.

Walking, sitting, standing still,
 I'm never tired, got that will,
 I saturate the thirsty ground,
 Like universal rain comes down.
Noble, lowly, rich and poor,
 Those who keep rules, those who ignore,
 Those with dignity complete,
 Those stumbling on unsteady feet.
Right views, wrong views, sharp and dull,
 I rain on partial, rain on full,
 Dharma rain comes equally,
 Never weary, ever free.

Now listen to what happens when they hear...
All those beings hear my song,
 According to where they belong,
 According to their capacity,
 They dwell on grounds of their degree.
Some stay among the gods on high,
 Or wheel-turning kings beneath the sky,
 Śakra or Brahma in their throne—
 These are small herbs that have grown.
Those who know unconditioned truth,
 Those who found nirvana's proof,
 With six powers and wisdom three,
 Living in mountains wild and free,
Always meditating deep,
 Pratyekabuddha's truth to keep—
 These are medium herbs that grow,
 Middle ground is what they know.

Those who seek the Buddha's place,
 Saying "We'll attain that grace,"
 Practicing with diligent mind—
 These superior herbs you'll find.
And those Buddha-sons so bright,
 Focusing on Buddha's light,
 Always practicing compassion's way,
 Knowing they'll be Buddhas someday,
Certain, without any doubt,
 That's what small trees are about.
 Those dwelling in supernatural might,
 Turning wheels that shine so bright,
Saving countless beings here,
 Millions saved year after year,
 These bodhisattvas strong and true—
 These are great trees breaking through.

The Buddha preaches equally, y'all...
Buddha preaches equally,
 Like rain of single quality,
 But according to each nature's way,
 What they receive ain't the same each day.
Like the plants and all the trees,
 Each one drinking as it please,
 Buddha uses parables so fine,
 To reveal and show the true design.
With various words and various ways,
 He expounds one Dharma all his days,
 But compared to Buddha's wisdom deep,
 It's like one drop the ocean keeps.

I rain the Dharma everywhere,
 Filling this world with loving care,
 Dharma of a single taste,
 Practiced according to each one's pace.
Like the shrubs so small and low,
 Healing herbs beginning to grow,
 All the trees both great and small,
 According to their size, they all—
They all grow lush and beautiful,
 Every single one gets full,
 Dharma of the Buddhas true,
 Always single, always new.
It causes all this world around,
 To be universally complete and sound,
 Gradually they cultivate their soul,
 All attain the Way's sweet goal.

Śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas too...
Śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas dwelling,
 In mountain forests, story telling,
 In their final body here,
 Hear the Dharma, fruit appears.
These are called the healing plants,
 Each one growing through their stance,
 And bodhisattvas wise and strong,
 Whose wisdom's firm their whole life long,
Understanding three realms clear,
 Seeking supreme vehicle here—
 These are called the small trees tall,
 Growing, flourishing one and all.

Those who dwell in meditation deep,
 Supernatural powers they keep,
 Hearing all Dharmas empty be,
 Hearts filled with joy and ecstasy.
They emit countless lights so bright,
 Saving beings left and right,
 These are called the great trees strong,
 Growing, flourishing all along.

So Kāśyapa, hear this truth...
Thus Kāśyapa, hear me say,
 The Dharma preached the Buddha's way,
 Is like a great cloud mighty and tall,
 With rain of one taste for us all,
Nourishing humanity's flower,
 In every moment, every hour,
 So each can come to fruition sweet,
 Each can make the Way complete.
Kāśyapa, you need to know,
 With various causes as I go,
 With various parables I share,
 I reveal the Buddha Way with care.
This is my expedient means,
 Greater than it ever seems,
 And all the Buddhas work this way,
 This is what I've got to say.

Now here's the final truth, the most real...
I'm telling you the truthful word,
 Most truthful thing you've ever heard:
 The assembly of śrāvakas here,
 Haven't truly disappeared.
The path that you've been walking on,
 Is the Bodhisattva Way, withdrawn,
 You'll gradually learn and cultivate,
 You'll all become Buddhas—that's your fate!

Let it rain, let it rain
 One sweet Dharma rain
 Let it fall, let it fall
 On the great and small
 Every plant gonna grow
 Every seed gonna show
 We're all becoming Buddhas
 That's the way it goes
Amen, Amen, Amen

Chapter 6: Bestowal of Prophecies

At that time, the World-Honored One, having spoken this verse, said to the great assembly, and proclaimed: “My disciple Mahākāśyapa, in a future lifetime, will see and serve three million million Buddhas, make offerings to them, show respect, reverence, and praise, and will widely preach the immeasurable great Dharma of the Buddhas. In his last body, he will become a Buddha, named Light Tathagata, Worthy One, Truly and Universally Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Supreme Being, Tamer of Men, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. His land will be named Virtue-Light, and the kalpa will be named Great Adornment. The Buddha's life span will be twelve minor kalpas. The True Dharma will remain in the world for twenty minor kalpas, and the Dharma-Image will also remain for twenty minor kalpas. His land will be adorned, without any filth, rocks, tiles, thorns, or uncleanliness. The land will be level, without any hills, depressions, pits, or mounds. The ground will be of lapis lazuli, with rows of jeweled trees, and golden ropes will line the sides of the roads. Various precious flowers will be scattered everywhere, and it will be completely pure. In his land, the Bodhisattvas will be immeasurable thousands of millions, and the śrāvaka assembly will also be countless. There will be no demonic affairs, and although there will be demons and their followers, they will all protect the Buddha’s Dharma.”
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
I tell all the monks, with my Buddha eye so clear,
I see this Kasyapa, banishin' all fear.
In a future life, after countless kalpas have rolled,
He'll attain Buddhahood, a story to be told.
He'll make offerings and serve, three million million strong,
World-Honored Buddhas, where he truly does belong.
For the Buddha's wisdom, his good conduct will shine,
Pure and steady, on that spiritual line.
After he's offered to the supreme, Double-Footed Honored Ones,
He'll cultivate all that wisdom, till his work is done.
In his last body, he'll attain that holy light,
His land will be pure, lapis lazuli, shinin' ever so bright.
Many jeweled trees will line the roadsides wide,
Golden ropes will guide you, with nowhere left to hide.
Those who see them will rejoice, a pleasant fragrance in the air,
Many famous flowers scattered, beyond all compare.
All kinds of wondrous things, subtle and so grand,
Will serve as adornments, in that promised land.

His ground will be level, no hills or pits you'll find,
Bodhisattva assemblies, leavin' no one behind.
Their minds gentle and subdued, with great powers they'll ascend,
Upholdin' Mahayana scriptures, right until the end.
The Sravaka assemblies, in their last body, free from stain,
The sons of the King of Dharma, a countless, holy train.
Even with the heavenly eye, you couldn't count them all,
That Buddha's life span, twelve minor kalpas, standin' strong and tall.

The True Dharma will remain, twenty minor kalpas, hear my plea,
And the Dharma-Image too, for twenty more, you see.
Light Tathagata's story, like this, the blues will tell,
A future shining bright, where all good souls will dwell.
At that time, Mahāmaudgalyāyana, Subhūti, and Mahākātyāyana, were all filled with awe. With a single mind, they joined their palms, looked up at the revered face of the Buddha, and did not take their eyes off him. They spoke together in verse, saying:
Oh, great and mighty Buddha, strong and true,
King of Dharma, shining through and through.
From your deep compassion, a voice we hear,
Whispering wisdom, chasing away all fear.
If you could see deep down, where our hearts reside,
And know that prophecy, we can't deny,
Like sweet cool dew on a summer's day,
Washing our troubles and our heat away.

Like folks from a land where hunger bites hard,
Seein' a king's grand feast, a lavish card.
Still got a tremor, a doubt in our soul,
Too scared to eat, not yet makin' us whole.

But if the king says, "Go on, take a bite!"
Then we'd dig in, with all of our might.
