At that time, the two thousand learners and non-learners, having heard the Buddha's prophecy, were filled with joy and delight and spoke in verse:
The World-Honored One, the lamp of wisdom bright,
We heard your prophecy, shinin' in the night.
Our hearts are filled with joy, it's plain to see,
Like sweet dew anointed, settin' our spirits free.



Chapter 10: A Teacher of the Dharma
At that time, the World-Honored One, addressing Bodhisattva Medicine King, said to the eighty thousand great beings: "Medicine King, do you see in this great assembly the immeasurable gods, dragon kings, yakshas, gandharvas, asuras, garudas, kinnaras, mahoragas, humans, non-humans, and monks, nuns, laymen, and laywomen? There are also those who seek the śrāvaka path, those who seek the pratyekabuddha path, and those who seek the Buddha Way. All of them, in my presence, who hear even one verse or one line of the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma, or even rejoice for a single thought, I will give them all a prophecy that they will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
The Buddha said to Medicine King: "Furthermore, after the Tathagata's passing away, if there is a person who hears even one verse or one line of the Lotus Sutra and rejoices for a single thought, I will also give them a prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. If there is a person who upholds, reads, recites, explains, or copies the Lotus Sutra, even a single verse, and reveres this scripture as if it were the Buddha himself, making various offerings—of flowers, incense, garlands, powdered incense, paste incense, burning incense, silken canopies, banners, streamers, robes, and music—and even joins their palms in reverence, Medicine King, you should know that these people have already made offerings to a hundred thousand million Buddhas in the past. Under those Buddhas, they have fulfilled their great vows and, out of compassion for sentient beings, are born in this human world."
"Medicine King, if someone asks what kind of sentient beings will attain Buddhahood in a future life, you should show them these people and say that they will surely attain Buddhahood in a future life. Why? If a noble man or noble woman upholds, reads, recites, explains, or copies even a single line of the Lotus Sutra, and makes various offerings to the sutra scrolls—of flowers, incense, garlands, powdered incense, paste incense, burning incense, silken canopies, banners, streamers, robes, and music, and joins their palms in reverence—this person should be looked up to by the entire world. He should be offered the same kind of offerings that are made to a Tathagata. You should know that this person is a great Bodhisattva who has fulfilled Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi and, out of compassion for sentient beings, has vowed to be born in this world to widely preach and differentiate the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma. How much greater is the merit for one who can fully uphold it and make all kinds of offerings?"
"Medicine King, you should know that this person, leaving the reward of his pure karma behind, is born into this evil world after my passing away, out of compassion for sentient beings, to widely preach this sutra. If this noble man or noble woman, after my passing away, is able to secretly preach the Lotus Sutra, even a single line, to one other person, you should know that this person is the Tathagata's envoy, sent by the Tathagata to do the Tathagata's work. How much more so for one who widely preaches it to a great assembly?"
"Medicine King, if there is an evil person who, with an unwholesome mind, constantly slanders and abuses the Buddha in the Buddha's presence for an entire kalpa, his sin is still light. But if a person, with a single evil word, slanders a layman or laywoman, or a monk or nun, who reads and recites the Lotus Sutra, their sin is much heavier."
"Medicine King, if a person reads and recites the Lotus Sutra, you should know that this person is adorning himself with the Buddha's adornments and is being carried on the Tathagata's shoulders. Wherever he goes, people should follow him and pay reverence, join their palms with a single mind, and make offerings to him with reverence, respect, and praise, with flowers, incense, garlands, powdered incense, paste incense, burning incense, silken canopies, banners, streamers, robes, fine food, and music. They should make the most supreme offerings of humans to him. They should hold heavenly treasures and scatter them, and heavenly jewels should be offered to him. Why? Because this person preaches the Dharma with joy, and by listening to it for a moment, one can attain ultimate Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If you want to walk the Buddha Way, child,
And find that wisdom, self-enlightened,
You gotta make your offerings, keep 'em strong and mild,
And hold that Lotus Sutra, day and night, unbrightened.

Now if you're lookin' for that All-Knowing mind,
Gotta hold this sutra close, you see,
And give to those who hold it, the generous, kind,
For they're preachin' truth for you and me.

Yeah, if there's one who can uphold, with a steady hand,
That Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma, deep and true,
You know they're the Buddha's envoy, across the land,
Thinkin' of all beings, in shades of blues and gold, it's true.

All who can hold that Lotus Sutra, pure and bright,
They leave their heaven, so sweet and calm,
And they're born in this evil world, through the darkest night,
Out of compassion for others, keepin' us safe from harm.

You oughta know, such a soul, they're free to roam,
Wherever their spirit calls, they can be found,
And in this troubled world, they'll make their home,
Preachin' the highest Dharma, on holy ground.

So use your heavenly flowers, let the incense rise,
And those jeweled robes, from skies above,
And the wonderful treasures, before your very eyes,
To offer to the preacher, with all your love.

After I'm gone, in this world so hard and mean,
Those who can hold this sutra, strong and bold,
Greet 'em with joined palms, a reverent scene,
Like you're offerin' to the World-Honored, stories yet untold.

With fine food, sweet delicacies, a feast for the soul,
And robes of every color, a beautiful sight,
Offer to this Buddha's son, makin' him whole,
Just hopin' for a moment of Dharma, bathed in golden light.

If there's one in a future age, listen close and hear,
Who can hold this sutra, brave and grand,
I'll send him among humans, dispellin' every fear,
To do the Tathagata's work, across the troubled land.

Now for a whole long kalpa, if your mind ain't straight,
And with an angry face, you curse the Buddha's name,
You'll gather heavy sins, seals of a bitter fate,
An immeasurable burden, a burning, endless flame.

But if a soul who reads, recites, and holds this holy text,
That Lotus Sutra, precious and divine,
Is slandered with a single word, troubled and perplexed,
That sin is even heavier, a burden more malign.

If there's a soul who seeks the Buddha Way, so grand,
And for a whole long kalpa, with palms held high,
Joins 'em in my presence, across the land,
Praisin' me with countless verses, beneath the sky.

Because of this praise, the Buddha's name so sweet,
They'll gain immeasurable merits, a blessed reward,
But the merit of praisin' the one who holds this sutra, complete,
Is even greater, by far, revered and adored.

For eighty million kalpas, with colors bright and bold,
And the most wonderful sounds, a symphony to hear,
With smells and tastes and touches, stories to be told,
They make their offerings to the sutra holder, year after year.

After makin' such offerings, humble and sincere,
If they hear a moment of the Dharma, oh so sweet,
They oughta congratulate themselves, banishin' every fear,
"I've gained a great benefit now," from my head down to my feet.

Medicine King, I'm tellin' you now, listen to what I say,
Of all the sutras I have preached, near and far,
Among them, the Lotus Sutra, it lights the way,
The most supreme, my friend, like a shining, guiding star.
At that time, the Buddha again said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Medicine King: "Of the immeasurable millions of sutras that I have preached, am now preaching, and will preach, among them, this Lotus Sutra is the most difficult to believe and difficult to comprehend. Medicine King, this sutra is the secret treasury of the Buddhas. It should not be distributed or casually given to others. It is protected by the World-Honored Buddhas and has never been revealed until now. But even when the Tathagata is in the world, this sutra still draws many grudges and jealousies. How much more so after my passing away?"
"Medicine King, you should know that after the Tathagata's passing, anyone who is able to copy, uphold, read, recite, make offerings to, and preach this sutra to others, will be covered by the Tathagata’s robe and will be protected and remembered by the present Buddhas of the other directions. This person has great power of faith, the power of a great vow, and the power of all good roots. You should know that this person dwells with the Tathagata and that the Tathagata's hand has touched his head."
"Medicine King, in every place where this sutra is preached, read, recited, or copied, or wherever a scroll of this sutra is kept, you should erect a stupa of seven treasures. It should be made extremely tall, vast, and beautifully adorned. There is no need to place relics inside. Why? Because the full body of the Tathagata is already there. This stupa should be revered and worshipped with all kinds of flowers, incense, garlands, silken canopies, banners, streamers, music, and songs. If a person sees this stupa, bows, and makes offerings to it, you should know that these people are all close to Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
"Medicine King, there are many people, both lay and monastic, who practice the Bodhisattva Way. If they are unable to see, hear, read, recite, copy, uphold, or make offerings to this Lotus Sutra, you should know that these people have not yet properly practiced the Bodhisattva Way. But if a person is able to hear this scripture, they are then able to properly practice the Bodhisattva Way. If there are sentient beings who seek the Buddha Way and who see or hear this Lotus Sutra, and having heard it, believe in it, understand it, and uphold it, you should know that this person is close to Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
"Medicine King, it is like a person who is thirsty and needs water. In a high, dry plateau, he digs and searches for it. If he still sees dry soil, he knows the water is still far away. But he continues to work, and when he starts to see wet soil, and then gradually mud, his mind is certain that the water is near. A Bodhisattva is also like this. If they have not heard, understood, or been able to practice this Lotus Sutra, you should know that this person is still far from Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. But if they can hear, understand, contemplate, and practice it, they will surely know that they are close to Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. Why? All the Bodhisattvas' Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi belongs to this sutra. This sutra opens the gate of expedient means and reveals the true nature of reality. This treasury of the Lotus Sutra is profound, firm, and remote, and no one can reach it. But now, the Buddha, having converted and brought the Bodhisattvas to completion, is revealing it for them."
"Medicine King, if a Bodhisattva hears this Lotus Sutra and is shocked, doubtful, and afraid, you should know that he is a newly resolved Bodhisattva. If a śrāvaka hears this sutra and is shocked, doubtful, and afraid, you should know that he is arrogant. Medicine King, if a noble man or noble woman, after the Tathagata’s passing, wishes to preach this Lotus Sutra to the four groups of the assembly, how should they do it? This noble man or noble woman should enter the Tathagata’s room, wear the Tathagata’s robe, and sit on the Tathagata’s seat. Only then should they widely preach this sutra to the four groups. The Tathagata’s room is a mind of great compassion for all sentient beings. The Tathagata’s robe is a mind of gentleness and patience. The Tathagata’s seat is the emptiness of all phenomena. When one dwells in this, then with a tireless mind, one should widely preach this Lotus Sutra for the Bodhisattvas and the four groups."
"Medicine King, in other lands, I will send a phantom person to gather an assembly to listen to the Dharma. I will also send phantom monks, nuns, laymen, and laywomen to listen to him preach the Dharma. These phantom people, when they hear the Dharma, will believe it, accept it, and follow it without opposition. If the preacher is in a secluded place, I will then send gods, dragons, ghost-spirits, gandharvas, asuras, and others in great numbers to listen to his Dharma. Although I am in another world, I will, from time to time, cause the preacher to see my body. If he forgets a phrase or a line, I will preach it to him again, so he can have it completely."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If you wanna shake off that lazy haze,
Gotta lend an ear to these ancient ways.
This sutra's hard, it ain't for the faint of heart,
Few believe, fewer still play a righteous part.

Like a thirsty soul on a dry, high plain,
Diggin' a narrow well, feelin' the strain.
Seein' naught but dust, he knows deep down,
That life-givin' water ain't yet in this town.

But when that earth gets damp, that mud appears,
He knows the water's close, calmin' his fears.
Medicine King, you gotta understand,
Folks are the same, wanderin' this land.

If they ain't heard the Lotus, a long, long road,
Far from the Buddha's wisdom, a heavy load.
But when this deep sutra rings in their ears,
The śrāvakas' Dharma, it washes away tears.

This is the king, yes, of all the lore,
Hear it, and ponder, right to your core.
Know these folks, with a soul so true,
Are close to the Buddha's wisdom, me and you.

If you preach this sutra, with a heart so bold,
Enter the Tathagata's room, stories untold.
Wear his gentle robe, on his seat you'll rest,
In the assembly, fearless, standin' the test.

Preach the Dharma wide, let it spread like a song,
Great compassion's the room, where you truly belong.
Gentleness, patience, that's the robe you wear,
Emptiness of all things, the seat beyond compare.
Dwelling in this truth, the Dharma you'll teach,
If slanders come your way, beyond your reach.

Evil words, or a knife, a stick, or a stone,
Endure it, child, for the Buddha's throne.
I'll appear, pure and firm, in lands far and wide,
Millions of millions, with nowhere to hide.

For kalpas untold, I'll preach till the end,
For all sentient beings, a true, faithful friend.
After I'm gone, if one preaches this word,
My phantom forms I'll send, for all to be heard.

Monks, nuns, laymen, and laywomen too,
Making offerings, for the teacher, so true.
Guiding all beings, gathering them near,
To listen to Dharma, banishing all fear.

If evil folks come, with a knife or a stick,
My phantom people I'll send, to protect them quick.
If the preacher's alone, in a place so still,
No human sound, just the breeze on the hill.

Reading, reciting, with a peaceful mind,
I'll appear for him then, a vision defined.
Pure and luminous body, a light in the dark,
If a phrase is forgotten, I'll leave my mark.

I'll preach it again, make it crystal clear,
If one has these merits, banish all fear.
Preaching to four groups, or in quiet space,
They'll all see my body, by my grace.

If a soul is secluded, with no one around,
Kings of gods and dragons, I'll send to that ground.
Yakshas, ghost-spirits, a silent, deep throng,
To be his assembly, where they all belong.

This soul will delight, preaching with ease,
Differentiating Dharma, bringing hearts to peace.
Protected by Buddhas, remembered so well,
Making the assembly joyful, casting a spell.
Close to a Dharma teacher, a path so bright,
Quickly attaining Bodhisattva's light.
Following this teacher, learning the way,
Seein' Buddhas like Ganges sands, every day.




Chapter 11: The Appearance of the Jeweled Stupa
At that time, in front of the Buddha, a seven-jeweled stupa, five hundred yojanas high and two hundred and fifty yojanas in both length and width, rose from the ground and hung in the air. It was adorned with various treasures. It had five thousand railings and thousands of millions of pavilions. It was adorned with countless banners and streamers, and hundreds of thousands of millions of precious bells and jeweled garlands hung from it. From its four sides, it emitted the fragrance of tāmalapattra and sandalwood, which filled the world.
The banners and canopies were made of gold, silver, lapis lazuli, tridacna, agate, pearls, and rose quartz—the seven treasures—and they reached up to the palaces of the Four Heavenly Kings. The gods of the Thirty-three Heavens rained down heavenly maṇḍārava flowers to make offerings to the jeweled stupa. The other gods, dragons, yakshas, gandharvas, asuras, garudas, kinnaras, mahoragas, humans, and non-humans, numbering in the millions and millions, made offerings to the jeweled stupa with all kinds of flowers, incense, garlands, banners, canopies, and music. They showed reverence, respect, and praise.
At that time, a great voice came from within the jeweled stupa, praising: "Excellent! Excellent, Śākyamuni World-Honored One! You are able to preach this Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma for the great assembly, which is the Dharma of the Bodhisattvas, protected and remembered by the Buddhas, with your equal and great wisdom. It is just so, it is just so, Śākyamuni World-Honored One. All that you have preached is true."
At that time, the four groups of the assembly saw the great jeweled stupa hanging in the air and heard the voice coming from within it. They were all filled with Dharma joy and astonished by this unprecedented event. They rose from their seats, respectfully joined their palms, and stood to one side.
At that time, a Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva named Great-Joyful-Eloquence, knowing the doubts in the hearts of all the gods, humans, and asuras in the world, said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, what is the reason for this jeweled stupa to have risen from the ground, and for this voice to have come from within it?"
At that time, the Buddha said to Bodhisattva Great-Joyful-Eloquence: "Within this jeweled stupa is the complete body of a Tathagata. It was in the past, in the east, in immeasurable millions of millions of asaṃkhyeya worlds, that there was a land named Jewel-Purity. In that land there was a Buddha named Abundant Treasures. When that Buddha practiced the Bodhisattva Way, he made a great vow: 'When I attain Buddhahood and pass away, my stupa will appear before any place in the ten directions where the Lotus Sutra is preached, to bear witness and to praise: "Excellent!"'"
"After that Buddha attained the Way, when he was about to pass away, he told all the monks in the assembly of gods and humans: 'After my passing away, if you wish to make offerings to my complete body, you should erect a great stupa.' With that Buddha's supernatural powers and vow, wherever in the worlds of the ten directions the Lotus Sutra is preached, that jeweled stupa appears before it, and his complete body is within the stupa, praising: 'Excellent! Excellent!'"
"Great-Joyful-Eloquence, this stupa of Abundant Treasures Tathagata has risen from the ground and is praising: 'Excellent! Excellent!' because it heard the preaching of the Lotus Sutra."
At that time, Bodhisattva Great-Joyful-Eloquence, by the Tathagata's supernatural power, said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, we wish to see this Buddha's body."
The Buddha said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Great-Joyful-Eloquence: "This Abundant Treasures Buddha has a profound and heavy vow. When my jeweled stupa appears before all the Buddhas for the sake of hearing the Lotus Sutra, if someone wishes to show my body to the four groups of the assembly, the Buddhas who are my emanations and are preaching the Dharma in the worlds of the ten directions must all be gathered in one place. Only then will my body appear."
"Great-Joyful-Eloquence, my emanations—the Buddhas who are preaching the Dharma in the worlds of the ten directions—should now be gathered."
Great-Joyful-Eloquence said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, we also wish to see the World-Honored One’s emanations, the Buddhas, to pay homage to them and make offerings."
At that time, the Buddha released a single ray of light from the white hair between his eyebrows and immediately saw the Buddhas of the five hundred million million nayuta worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges in the east. In those lands, the ground was made of crystal, adorned with jeweled trees and jeweled robes. Innumerable millions of millions of Bodhisattvas filled them. They were covered everywhere with jeweled canopies and jeweled nets were spread above. The Buddhas in those lands preached the various Dharmas with a great and wonderful voice, and countless millions of millions of Bodhisattvas filled all those lands, preaching the Dharma for the assembly. The places illuminated by the light from the white hair in the south, north, west, the four diagonals, and above and below were also like this.
At that time, all the Buddhas of the ten directions each said to their assembly of Bodhisattvas: "Noble men, we should now go to the Saha world, to Śākyamuni Buddha’s place, and also make offerings to the jeweled stupa of Abundant Treasures Tathagata."
At that time, the Saha world transformed and became pure. The ground was of lapis lazuli, adorned with jeweled trees, and golden ropes lined the eight roads. There were no villages, towns, cities, great oceans, rivers, mountains, streams, or forests. A great, precious fragrance was burned, and maṇḍārava flowers were spread all over the ground. A jeweled net canopy was spread above, and precious bells hung from it. Only the assembly that was there was left. The gods and humans were moved to other lands.
At that time, the Buddhas, each with one great Bodhisattva as an attendant, arrived in the Saha world, and each came to a jeweled tree. Each jeweled tree was five hundred yojanas high, and its branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits were adorned in a beautiful order. Under each of the jeweled trees was a lion’s seat, five yojanas high, also decorated with great treasures. At that time, the Buddhas each sat in the lotus posture on these seats. They spread out in this way and filled the entire three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world. But the emanations of Śākyamuni Buddha from a single direction were still not exhausted.
Śākyamuni Buddha, wishing to make room for his emanations, the Buddhas, transformed two hundred million million nayuta worlds in each of the eight directions, making them all pure. There were no hells, hungry ghosts, animals, or asuras. He also moved the gods and humans and placed them in other lands. These transformed lands also had a ground of lapis lazuli, were adorned with jeweled trees, and the trees were five hundred yojanas high. Their branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits were adorned in a beautiful order. Under each tree was a jeweled lion’s seat, five yojanas high, also decorated with various treasures. There were also no great oceans, rivers, or the kings of mountains like Mount Mucilinda, Mount Mahāmucilinda, the Iron Ring Mountains, the Great Iron Ring Mountains, or Mount Sumeru. It all became one Buddha land. The jeweled ground was level, and a jeweled net canopy covered it everywhere. Banners and canopies hung down, a great, precious fragrance was burned, and all kinds of heavenly jeweled flowers were spread all over the ground.
Śākyamuni Buddha, for the sake of the Buddhas who were to come and sit, again transformed two hundred million million nayuta worlds in each of the eight directions, making them all pure. There were no hells, hungry ghosts, animals, or asuras. He also moved the gods and humans and placed them in other lands. These transformed lands also had a ground of lapis lazuli, were adorned with jeweled trees, and the trees were five hundred yojanas high. Their branches, leaves, flowers, and fruits were adorned in a beautiful order. Under each tree was a jeweled lion’s seat, five yojanas high, also decorated with various treasures. There were also no great oceans, rivers, or the kings of mountains like Mount Mucilinda, Mount Mahāmucilinda, the Iron Ring Mountains, the Great Iron Ring Mountains, or Mount Sumeru. It all became one Buddha land. The jeweled ground was level, and a jeweled net canopy covered it everywhere. Banners and canopies hung down, a great, precious fragrance was burned, and all kinds of heavenly jeweled flowers were spread all over the ground.
At that time, the Buddhas who were the emanations of Śākyamuni Buddha from the hundreds of thousands of millions of nayuta worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges in the east, each came here after preaching the Dharma. In this way, the Buddhas of the ten directions all came and gathered, sitting in the eight directions. At that time, each direction was filled with four hundred million million nayuta Tathagatas. At that time, the Buddhas, each sitting on a lion’s seat under a jeweled tree, all sent their attendants to ask Śākyamuni Buddha about his well-being. They each brought a handful of jeweled flowers and said:
"Noble man, please go to Śākyamuni Buddha at Mount Gridhrakuta and greet him on my behalf: 'Are you in good health and without much sickness? Are you at ease in your energy? Are the Bodhisattva and śrāvaka assemblies also at peace?' Scatter these jeweled flowers as an offering to the Buddha and say: 'The Buddha of a certain land, wishes to open this jeweled stupa.'" The envoys of all the other Buddhas also did the same.
At that time, Śākyamuni Buddha, seeing that all his emanations had gathered and were all sitting on a lion’s seat, and hearing all the Buddhas wish to open the jeweled stupa, rose from his seat and stood in the air. The entire four groups of the assembly rose, joined their palms, and looked at the Buddha with a single mind.
Then, Śākyamuni Buddha, with his right finger, opened the door of the seven-jeweled stupa. A great sound was heard, like removing a bolt and opening a great city gate. Immediately, the entire assembly saw Abundant Treasures Tathagata sitting on a lion’s seat inside the jeweled stupa, his complete body intact as if he were in meditation. They also heard his words: "Excellent! Excellent, Śākyamuni Buddha! You have preached the Lotus Sutra so wonderfully. I have come here to hear this sutra."
At that time, the four groups of the assembly and others saw the Buddha who had passed away countless millions of kalpas ago speak these words. They praised this unprecedented event and scattered a shower of heavenly jeweled flowers upon Abundant Treasures Buddha and Śākyamuni Buddha.
Then, Abundant Treasures Buddha, in the jeweled stupa, offered half of his seat to Śākyamuni Buddha and said: "Śākyamuni Buddha, you may sit on this seat." Immediately, Śākyamuni Buddha entered the stupa and sat on the other half of the seat, in the lotus posture. At that time, the great assembly saw the two Tathagatas sitting in the lotus posture on the lion's seat in the seven-jeweled stupa. They all thought: "The Buddha's seat is high and far away. We only wish that the Tathagatas, with their supernatural powers, would cause us to also be in the air."
Immediately, Śākyamuni Buddha, with his supernatural power, took the great assembly and placed them all in the air. With a great voice, he universally announced to the four groups: "Who, in this Saha world, is able to widely preach the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma? The time is now. The Tathagata will soon enter nirvana, and the Buddha wishes to entrust this Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma to a worthy person."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
The holy master, World-Honored One, though he passed away so long ago,
Dwells in the jeweled stupa, and for the Dharma, he does show.
So why you all ain't diligent, for the Dharma, don't you see?
This Buddha left this world, for kalpas, endlessly.

He came to every corner, just to hear the Dharma sound,
'Cause a precious thing like this, ain't often to be found.
His vow was strong and clear, after my spirit takes its flight,
Wherever this sutra's preached, I'll be there day and night.

And all my emanations, Buddhas by the countless throng,
Like sands of old man Ganges, they've been comin' all along,
To listen to the Dharma, and to witness, it is true,
The Abundant Treasures Tathagata, passed away for me and you.

They left their wondrous lands, their disciples, and their kin,
Gods, humans, dragon-spirits, offerings they brought right in.
They came to make the Dharma, last for ages, strong and deep,
I moved assemblies, countless souls, the land to cleanse and keep.

The Buddhas found their seats, beneath a jeweled tree,
Like a pond of pure lotus, for all the world to see.
Underneath those branches, on the lion's mighty throne,
The Buddhas sit in splendor, with a light that's all their own.

Like a great torch burning bright, in the dark of night's embrace,
A wondrous scent surrounds them, fills all of time and space.
Sentient beings who breathe it, joy so deep they can't control,
Like a strong wind on a small tree, stirring up the very soul.

With this expedient means, the Dharma's made to stay,
I tell this great assembly, after I pass away,
Who will uphold, read, and preach this sutra, strong and bold?
Now, before the Buddha, let your sacred vows unfold.

Abundant Treasures Buddha, though he passed so long ago,
With his mighty vow, a lion's roar, he lets his wisdom flow.
The Abundant Treasures Tathagata, and this body you see here,
And all the emanations gathered, make their true intention clear.

All you sons of Buddha, who will stand to guard the way?
You must make a mighty vow, to make it last another day.
Those who can uphold this Dharma, this sutra, pure and grand,
Will be making offerings, to me and Abundant Treasures in this land.

This Abundant Treasures Buddha, in his jeweled stupa dwells,
Always travels ten directions, for this sutra, all it tells.
He makes his offerings also, to the emanations gathered near,
Adorns them with his light, in every world, banishing all fear.

If a person preaches this sutra, it's like they're seeing me,
Abundant Treasures Tathagata, and emanations, wild and free.
All you noble men and women, contemplate with thoughtful mind,
This is a task so heavy, a great vow you're bound to find.

All the other scriptures, like the Ganges' sands they roam,
Though one may preach them, it ain't like bringin' this one home.
If one could take Mount Sumeru, and throw it to another shore,
Countless Buddha lands beyond, that ain't difficult, no more.

If one could move the great wide world, with just a single toe,
And throw it to a distant land, that ain't difficult, you know.
If one could stand in highest heaven, preach for an assembly's ear,
Countless other sutras, that ain't difficult, never fear.

But if, after the Buddha's gone, in an age of evil's sway,
One can preach this sutra, that's the difficult task today.
If a person grabs the empty space, and walks upon the air,
That ain't difficult at all, beyond all earthly care.

After my passing, if you can copy, hold this sutra tight,
Or cause another soul to copy, that's the difficult light.
If one could place the great wide earth, upon a fingernail's tip,
And climb to Brahma heavens, that ain't no difficult trip.

After the Buddha's passing, in an age that's dark and grim,
If one can read this sutra, just for a moment, on a whim,
That's the difficult task, my friends, the hardest you will face.
If, in the great conflagration, with dry grass, you find your place,

And walk right in, without a burn, that ain't no difficult deed.
After my passing, if you can hold this sutra, plant this seed,
And preach it to just one soul, that's the difficult, it's true.
If one upholds the eighty-four thousand Dharmas, through and through,

Twelve divisions of the scriptures, expound them for all to see,
Cause all who listen, gain six powers, wild and magically,
Although one can do this, that ain't difficult, not a bit.
After my passing, if you can hear and take this sutra, lit,

And ask about its meaning, that's the difficult you crave.
If a person preaches Dharma, and countless beings they can save,
Hundreds, thousands, millions, like the Ganges' flowing sand,
To attain arhatship, with six powers in their hand,

Although there's all this benefit, that ain't difficult, not by far.
After my passing, if one can uphold these kinds of scriptures, star
Of all your deeds and efforts, that's the difficult, indeed.
I, for the Buddha Way, in countless lands, planted every seed.

From the beginning till right now, I've preached the sutras, far and wide,
But among them all, this sutra, is the first, with naught to hide.
If a person can uphold it, they hold the Buddha's very frame.
All you noble men and women, after I call out my name,

Who will receive, uphold, read, recite this sutra, strong and free?
Now, in the Buddha's presence, make your solemn vow to me.
This sutra's hard to hold onto, if for a moment, you succeed,
I will rejoice, and all the Buddhas, will truly heed.

Such a person, praised by Buddhas, every single one,
This is the brave and fierce one, when the day is done.
This is the diligent one, who keeps the precepts, practices with might,
He will quickly gain the Buddha Way, shining ever bright.

One who's able, in a future age, to read and hold this text,
Is a true son of the Buddha, on grounds of goodness, never vexed.
After the Buddha's passing, one who understands its soul,
Is the eye of gods and humans, and the world, making it whole.

In an age of fear and darkness, if he preaches, just a moment's span,
All the gods and humans, should make offerings to that man.


Chapter 12: Devadatta
At that time, the Buddha said to all the Bodhisattvas, gods, and the four groups of the assembly: "In the past, in immeasurable kalpas, I sought the Lotus Sutra without ever being lazy. For many kalpas, I was a king and made a vow to seek supreme enlightenment, and my mind did not waver. To fulfill the six pāramitās, I diligently practiced giving with a mind that was not stingy. I gave away my elephants, horses, seven treasures, country, cities, wife, and children, as well as my slaves and servants. I even gave away my head, eyes, marrow, brain, body, flesh, hands, and feet, and did not begrudge my life.
The people of that time had immeasurable life spans. For the sake of the Dharma, I gave up my royal position and entrusted the government to my crown prince. I had drums beaten and a proclamation sent in all four directions to seek the Dharma: 'Whoever can preach the Great Vehicle for me, I will be their servant and serve them for my entire life.' At that time, a hermit came and said to the king: 'I have a Great Vehicle sutra named the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma. If you do not oppose me, I will preach it to you.'
When the king heard the hermit's words, his heart was filled with joy and delight. He immediately followed the hermit and supplied all his needs. He picked fruit, drew water, gathered firewood, and prepared meals, and he even offered his own body as a bed. His body and mind were never weary. For a thousand years, he served the hermit. For the sake of the Dharma, he diligently attended to him, ensuring he had no lack."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
In a kalpa long ago, a king I used to be,
Not for worldly pleasures, but for truth, I longed to be free.
I sent my bells a-ringing, in the North, South, East, and West,
"Who holds the mighty Dharma? I'll be their servant, put me to the test."

Then came a hermit, Asita, with wisdom in his eyes,
"I know a Dharma subtle, a treasure to surprise.
If you can truly seek it, I'll preach it just for you,"
The king's heart swelled with gladness, his spirit shining through.

He followed that old hermit, with a humble, faithful soul,
Gathered wood and fruit, to make the wanderer whole.
His heart set on the Dharma, his body knew no pain,
Seeking truth for all beings, again and yet again.

Not for his own body, nor for pleasures that would fade,
But as a mighty monarch, a spiritual parade.
He found the great awakening, beneath a starlit sky,
And that's why I'm here singing, to tell you reasons why.
The Buddha said to all the monks: "The king at that time was myself. The hermit at that time is Devadatta today. It was because of the good friendship of Devadatta that I was able to fully complete the six pāramitās, compassion, joy, giving up, the thirty-two marks, the eighty subtle characteristics, the deep golden color, the ten powers, the four kinds of fearlessness, the four embracing Dharmas, the eighteen unique Dharmas, and the power of supernatural abilities. I attained true enlightenment and widely saved sentient beings, all because of the good friendship of Devadatta."
He said to the four groups of the assembly: "Devadatta, after an immeasurable number of kalpas from now, will attain Buddhahood. His title will be King of Heaven Tathagata, Worthy One, Truly and Universally Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Supreme Being, Tamer of Men, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. His world will be named Heavenly Path.
At that time, King of Heaven Buddha will remain in the world for twenty middle kalpas, widely preaching the wonderful Dharma for sentient beings. Sentient beings as numerous as the sands of the Ganges will attain the fruit of an arhat. Immeasurable sentient beings will give rise to the mind of a pratyekabuddha. Sentient beings as numerous as the sands of the Ganges will give rise to the mind for the supreme Way, attain the patience of non-arising, and reach the state of non-retrogression."
"After King of Heaven Buddha passes into parinirvāṇa, the True Dharma will remain in the world for twenty middle kalpas. A seven-jeweled stupa will be erected for his complete body-relics, sixty yojanas high and forty yojanas in both length and width. All the gods and people will use various flowers, powdered incense, burning incense, paste incense, robes, garlands, banners, canopies, music, and songs to pay homage and make offerings to this wonderful seven-jeweled stupa. Immeasurable sentient beings will attain the fruit of an arhat. Immeasurable sentient beings will be awakened as pratyekabuddhas, and an inconceivable number of sentient beings will give rise to the mind of enlightenment and reach the state of non-retrogression."
The Buddha said to all the monks: "In the future, if there is a noble man or noble woman who hears this Devadatta chapter of the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma with a pure heart of faith and respect and without giving rise to doubt, they will not fall into the hells, the hungry ghost realm, or the animal realm. They will be born in the presence of Buddhas in the ten directions, and wherever they are born, they will always hear this sutra. If they are born among humans or gods, they will receive supreme and wonderful joy. If they are born in the presence of a Buddha, they will be born by transformation in a lotus flower."
The Story of the Dragon King’s Daughter
At that time, the Bodhisattva who had followed Abundant Treasures World-Honored One from the lower direction, named Accumulated Wisdom, said to Abundant Treasures Buddha: "We should now return to our home land."
Śākyamuni Buddha said to Accumulated Wisdom: "Noble man, please wait for a moment. Here is a Bodhisattva named Mañjuśrī whom you can meet and discuss the wonderful Dharma with. Then you can return to your homeland."
At that time, Mañjuśrī, sitting on a lotus flower with a thousand petals as large as a chariot wheel, along with the Bodhisattvas who came with him, also sitting on jeweled lotus flowers, naturally arose from the great ocean, from the dragon king Sāgara’s palace. They dwelt in the air and went to Mount Vulture Peak. They descended from their lotus flowers, went to the Buddha’s place, and bowed their heads in reverence at the feet of the two World-Honored Ones. Having completed their respects, they went to Accumulated Wisdom and greeted him, then sat to one side.
Bodhisattva Accumulated Wisdom asked Mañjuśrī: "How many sentient beings have you converted in the dragon palace?"
Mañjuśrī said: "The number is immeasurable, incalculable, beyond what can be expressed in words or measured by the mind. Just wait for a moment, and you will see and know for yourself."
Before he had finished speaking, countless Bodhisattvas, sitting on jeweled lotus flowers, arose from the ocean. They went to Mount Vulture Peak and dwelt in the air. All these Bodhisattvas were converted by Mañjuśrī. They were all complete in the practices of a Bodhisattva and were all discussing the six pāramitās. Those who were originally śrāvakas, who were in the air, spoke of the śrāvaka practices, but now they all practiced the great vehicle Dharma of emptiness.
Mañjuśrī said to Accumulated Wisdom: "My work of converting beings in the ocean is like this."
At that time, Bodhisattva Accumulated Wisdom spoke in verse:
Great wisdom, virtue, courage, and power,
Converted so many, hour after hour.
Now this whole big crowd, and me too, you see,
We done all witnessed, for eternity.

You lay out the truth, plain for us to hear,
Unveil the One Vehicle, makin' it clear.
You guide all the lost, with a gentle hand,
Bring 'em to bright enlightenment, across the land.
Mañjuśrī said: "In the ocean, I always preach the Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma."
Accumulated Wisdom asked Mañjuśrī: "This sutra is so profound and subtle, a treasure among all sutras, and it is rare in the world. Are there any sentient beings who, by diligently cultivating and practicing this sutra, can quickly attain Buddhahood?"
Mañjuśrī said: "There is the daughter of Sāgara, the dragon king. She is only eight years old, but she has sharp faculties and wisdom. She is skilled in knowing the faculties and practices of all sentient beings. She has attained dhāranīs, and all the profound and secret treasuries preached by the Buddhas, she can receive and uphold. She has entered deep meditation, thoroughly understood all phenomena, and in a single moment, gave rise to the mind of enlightenment, attained non-retrogression, and has unhindered eloquence. She has a mind of compassion for sentient beings just like a loving mother has for her baby. She is complete in all merits, and her heart and words are subtle, wonderful, and vast. She is compassionate, benevolent, and gentle, and her resolve is harmonious and elegant. She can reach enlightenment."
Bodhisattva Accumulated Wisdom said: "I see Śākyamuni Tathagata, who, for immeasurable kalpas, practiced difficult and painful austerities, accumulating merits and virtues to seek the Way of enlightenment, without ever stopping. I have looked at the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world, and there is not even a space the size of a mustard seed that is not a place where a Bodhisattva gave up his body and life for the sake of sentient beings. Only after this could he attain the Way of enlightenment. I do not believe that this girl can attain true enlightenment in a moment."
Before he had finished speaking, the Dragon King's daughter suddenly appeared before him. She bowed her head in reverence and then stood to one side, speaking in verse:
I know 'bout sin, yeah, and I know 'bout grace,
My light shines out, all over the place.
My body's pure, subtle and true,
Got thirty-two marks, and eighty more too.

Gods and humans, they all look my way,
Dragons and spirits, they bow and they pray.
All suffering souls, they come to my side,
Heard I'll reach enlightenment, nowhere to hide.

Only the Buddha, he can see,
This great teaching I'm bringin', for all to be free.
To save troubled souls, from their sorrow and pain,
I'm preachin' the Dharma, again and again.
At that time, Śāriputra said to the dragon girl: "Your belief that you will quickly attain the supreme Way is difficult to believe. Why? A woman's body is defiled and is not a vessel for the Dharma. How can you attain supreme enlightenment? The Buddha Way is vast and far away, and it is attained only after cultivating diligently for immeasurable kalpas and completing all the pāramitās. Furthermore, a woman's body has five hindrances: first, she cannot become a Brahma King; second, a Śakra; third, a Māra King; fourth, a wheel-turning holy king; and fifth, a Buddha. How can a woman's body quickly attain Buddhahood?"
At that time, the dragon girl had a precious jewel, worth the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world, which she held up and offered to the Buddha. The Buddha immediately accepted it. The dragon girl said to Bodhisattva Accumulated Wisdom and the venerable Śāriputra: "I offered the jewel, and the World-Honored One accepted it. Was that fast?"
They replied: "Very fast!"
The girl said: "With your supernatural power, observe my attainment of Buddhahood, which will be even faster than that."
At that moment, everyone in the assembly saw the dragon girl, in an instant, transform into a man. He was complete in the practices of a Bodhisattva, and he immediately went to the world of Stainless-Purity in the south, where he sat on a jeweled lotus flower and attained true enlightenment. He was complete with the thirty-two marks and the eighty subtle characteristics, and he universally preached the wonderful Dharma for all sentient beings in the ten directions.
At that time, the Bodhisattvas, śrāvakas, gods, dragons, eight kinds of non-human beings, and the humans and non-humans of the Saha world all saw the dragon girl from a distance, becoming a Buddha. He universally preached the Dharma to the assembly of humans and gods at that time. Their hearts were filled with great joy, and they all bowed in reverence from a distance. Immeasurable sentient beings heard the Dharma, understood it, and attained non-retrogression. Three thousand sentient beings attained the prophecy for the Way. The land of Stainless-Purity shook in six ways, and three thousand sentient beings of the Saha world attained the ground of non-retrogression. Three thousand sentient beings gave rise to the mind for enlightenment and received a prophecy. Bodhisattva Accumulated Wisdom, Śāriputra, and the entire assembly silently believed and accepted it.



Chapter 13: Encouragement for Upholding the Sutra
At that time, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Medicine King and Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Great-Joyful-Eloquence, together with their retinue of twenty thousand Bodhisattvas, all made this vow in the presence of the Buddha: "We only wish, World-Honored One, that you would not be concerned. After the Buddha's passing, we will receive, uphold, read, recite, and preach this scripture. In the evil age to come, sentient beings' roots of goodness will dwindle, and their arrogance will increase. They will be greedy for offerings and gains, and their unwholesome roots will grow. They will be far from liberation. Although they will be difficult to convert, we will give rise to great power of patience and will read, recite, uphold, preach, copy, and make various offerings to this sutra without begrudging our bodies or lives."
At that time, the five hundred Arhats in the assembly who had received prophecies said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, we also vow that we will widely preach this sutra in other lands."
And the eight thousand learners and non-learners who had received prophecies rose from their seats, joined their palms toward the Buddha, and made this vow: "World-Honored One, we also will widely preach this sutra in other lands. Why? Because the people in this Saha world are very evil, filled with arrogance, shallow in their merit, and full of anger, impurity, flattery, and deceitfulness in their hearts."
Prophecies for the Nuns
At that time, the Buddha's foster mother, Mahāprajāpatī Gautamī, the nun, with her retinue of six thousand nuns who were learners and non-learners, rose from her seat. With a single mind, she joined her palms, and looked up at the Buddha’s revered face without taking her eyes off him.
Then the World-Honored One said to Gautamī: "Why do you look at the Tathagata with a sorrowful expression? Do you think that I did not mention your name and give you a prophecy for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi?"
"Gautamī, I previously said that all the śrāvakas have already received a prophecy. But now, if you wish to know your prophecy, in a future life, you will be a great Dharma teacher in the Dharma of sixty-eight thousand million Buddhas. You, and the six thousand learners and non-learners who are nuns, will all be Dharma teachers. In this way, you will gradually complete the Bodhisattva Way and will attain Buddhahood, with the title All-Sentient-Beings-Rejoiced-to-See Tathagata, Worthy One, Truly and Universally Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Supreme Being, Tamer of Men, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. Gautamī, this All-Sentient-Beings-Rejoiced-to-See Buddha and the six thousand Bodhisattvas will all, in succession, receive a prophecy and attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
At that time, Yaśodharā, the nun and mother of Rāhula, thought: "The World-Honored One has not mentioned my name in the prophecies."
The Buddha said to Yaśodharā: "In a future life, in the Dharma of hundreds of thousands of millions of Buddhas, you will practice the Bodhisattva Way and be a great Dharma teacher, gradually completing the Buddha Way. In a pure land, you will attain Buddhahood, with the title King of a Thousand Million Lights Tathagata, Worthy One, Truly and Universally Enlightened, Perfect in Knowledge and Conduct, Well-Gone, Knower of the World, Supreme Being, Tamer of Men, Teacher of Gods and Humans, Buddha, World-Honored One. The Buddha’s life span will be immeasurable asaṃkhyeya kalpas."
At that time, Mahāprajāpatī Gautamī, and Yaśodharā, the nun, along with their retinues, were all filled with great, unprecedented joy. They then spoke in verse in the presence of the Buddha:
Our Guide, World-Honored One,
Brings peace to everyone.
Our prophecy now heard and clear,
Calms every heart, dispels all fear.
After the nuns spoke this verse, they said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, we also will be able to widely preach this sutra in other lands."
The Vow of the Bodhisattvas
At that time, the World-Honored One looked at the eighty million million nayuta Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas. These Bodhisattvas had all attained non-retrogression, turned the non-retrogressing Dharma wheel, and attained all the dhāranīs. They immediately rose from their seats, went to the Buddha's presence, and joined their palms with a single mind. They thought: "If the World-Honored One commands us to uphold and preach this sutra, we will, as commanded by the Buddha, widely preach this Dharma." They thought again: "The Buddha is now silent and does not command us. What should we do?"
Then the Bodhisattvas, respectfully following the Buddha's will and also wishing to fulfill their own original vows, made a lion's roar in the Buddha’s presence and made this vow:
“World-Honored One, after the passing of the Tathagata, we will travel and wander in all the worlds of the ten directions and will enable sentient beings to copy this sutra, uphold it, read and recite it, explain its meaning, practice it according to the Dharma, and remember it properly. This will all be due to the Buddha's majestic power. We only wish that the World-Honored One, from another land, would watch over us from afar."
At that time, all the Bodhisattvas together spoke in verse, saying:
After the Buddha's gone, a truth we never learned,
In this mean old world we face, with trouble all around,
We'll preach the Dharma wide, with grace that can't be burned.
Ignorant folks will rise, with spite in their cold eyes,
Slander and abuse us, morning, noon, and night,
With evil words and blows, they'll bring us down with lies,
But we'll endure it all, with patience and with might.

In this wicked world, some monks will talk so bold,
With false wisdom in their hearts, and flattery they'll spew,
Claimin' what they ain't achieved, stories to be told,
Their arrogance will swell, for all the world to view.

Some will live alone, in rags upon their back,
Claimin' the true Way, like banners torn and frayed,
But they despise the folks, who walk the common track,
Lost in their lonely pride, a game they've sadly played.

Attached to earthly gain, and offerings they preach the call,
Revered by all the world, standin' proud and tall,
Like they were Arhats true, with powers to enthral,
Their false piety, a deceptive, bitter gall.

These folks with evil minds, they'll stray from day to day,
Thinkin' 'bout worldly things, in shadows and in light,
Claimin' to be holy, in a cunning, twisted way,
They love to find our faults, and point 'em out with spite.

And they will speak these words, with tongues so sharp and keen,
"These monks, for gain and fame, are tangled in their greed,
They preach outside the path, a twisted, wicked scene,
They wrote this scripture themselves, to plant a bitter seed."

"They fool the world for fame, a selfish, sorry plight,"
To slander this here sutra, as the day turns into night,
They'll gather in a crowd, with all their hateful might,
Wishin' to destroy us, in the fading, dimming light.

They'll go to kings and lords, and Brahmins old and wise,
And other monks assembled, answerin' their call,
They'll slander us with evil, spreadin' wicked lies,
Sayin' we got wrong views, beneath a cloudy pall.

Who preach outside the path, a grave and heavy sin,
Because we love the Buddha, this burden we must bear,
Scornful words we'll hear, from deep within,
"You'll all become Buddhas!" banishin' all fear.

All such scornful words, we'll swallow with a sigh,
In this turbid, evil age, troubles we will face,
Evil ghosts will whisper low, as wicked winds pass by,
And slander and abuse us, without a moment's grace.

We'll wear the armor of patience, for the Buddha's holy name,
For preachin' this here sutra, with calm and steady hand,
We don't love our bodies, or this transient, fleeting flame,
But we cherish the supreme Way, shinin' through the land.

In a future life, we'll guard what Buddha did bestow,
The World-Honored One, He surely knows our trod,
The evil monks of this mean age, who just don't wanna know,
Buddha's expedient means, a sacred gift from God.

And His preachin' of the Dharma, so perfectly aligned,
Will bring abuse and frowns, from hearts so cold and mean,
They'll be banished from the stupas, and temples grand and kind,
All these evil things we'll bear, in this earthly, weary scene.

We endure, rememberin' Buddha's command, so deep and true,
In all the towns and cities, where weary souls do sleep,
Where folks are seekin' Dharma, we'll go and see it through,
To preach the Dharma Buddha gave, a promise we will keep.

We are the World-Honored One's envoys, with hearts so free and bold,
And we won't fear the assembly, no, not one little bit,
We'll skillfully preach the Dharma, with stories to be told,
We wish the Buddha would be at peace, in pure, clear light, where He sits.

In the presence of the Buddha, our hearts align and pray,
And all the Buddhas from ten directions, a sacred, holy sign,
We make this vow, with spirits strong, to light the darkest day,
The Buddha Himself knows our hearts, makin' us truly shine.


The Lotus Sutra of the Wonderful Dharma, Volume Five
Translated by Tripitaka Master Kumarajiva of the Kucha Kingdom in the Later Qin Dynasty, by imperial decree.



Chapter 14: Peaceful and Joyful Practices
At that time, the Dharma Prince Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Mañjuśrī said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, these Bodhisattvas are very rare. Out of reverence for the Buddha, they have made a great vow to protect, uphold, read, and preach this Lotus Sutra in the evil age to come. World-Honored One, how should the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas preach this sutra in the evil age to come?"
The Buddha said to Mañjuśrī: "If the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva wishes to preach this sutra in the evil age to come, he must dwell in four Dharmas. First, he must dwell in the sphere of actions and the sphere of proximity of a Bodhisattva, and then he can expound this sutra for sentient beings."
"Mañjuśrī, what is called the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva's sphere of actions? The Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva must dwell on the ground of patience, be gentle and yielding, without being abrupt, and their mind should not be agitated. Furthermore, for the Dharma, he should not take any action, but should contemplate all Dharmas as their true nature. He should not act or differentiate. This is called the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva's sphere of actions."
"What is called the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva's sphere of proximity? The Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva should not be close to kings, princes, ministers, or officials. He should not be close to heretics, Brahmins, or the Nirgranthas, or those who write worldly literature, who praise outside scriptures, or the Lokāyatas and their opponents. He also should not be close to those who engage in dangerous games, wrestling, or wrestling with animals, and all kinds of theatrical performances. He should also not be close to caṇḍālas, or those who raise pigs, sheep, chickens, or dogs, and those who hunt, fish, and engage in all unwholesome professions. If such people happen to come, he should preach the Dharma for them without any hope for a return. He should also not be close to or greet the monks, nuns, laymen, or laywomen who seek the śrāvaka path. Whether in their rooms, in their walking meditation area, or in the Dharma hall, he should not live with them. If they happen to come, he should preach the Dharma to them according to what is appropriate, without any hope for a return."
"Mañjuśrī, furthermore, the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva should not look at a woman in a way that gives rise to thoughts of desire and should preach the Dharma for them. He should also not take pleasure in seeing them. If he enters another person's home, he should not speak alone with young girls, virgins, or widows. He should also not be close to the five kinds of people who are not fully men. He should not enter another person's home alone. If there is a reason for him to enter alone, he should only think of the Buddha with a single mind. If he preaches the Dharma for a woman, he should not show his teeth in laughter, or show his chest. Even for the Dharma, he should not be too intimate, let alone for other matters. He should not take pleasure in having young disciples, śrāmaṇeras, or small children, or in having the same teacher as them. He should always enjoy sitting in meditation and, in a secluded place, cultivate and gather his mind. Mañjuśrī, this is called the first sphere of proximity."
"Furthermore, the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva should contemplate that all Dharmas are empty, just as their true nature is. They are not upside down, motionless, non-retrogressing, and unchangeable. They are like empty space, without any fixed nature. All verbal expressions are cut off. They do not arise, are not created, do not appear. They have no name and no form. They are truly without any fixed existence. They are immeasurable, boundless, unhindered, and without obstacles. They are only said to exist due to conditions, and they arise from upside-down views. He should always delight in contemplating the true nature of the Dharma. This is called the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva's second sphere of proximity."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If a Bodhisattva comes, in them evil days to be,
Without a fearful heart, a preacher of the truth for all to see,
He's gotta walk the righteous path, in action and in near,
Keepin' far from kings and princes, lettin' go of all his fear.

Ministers and officials, dangerous folk who like to play,
Caṇḍālas and the heretics, he'll turn and walk away.
Arrogant and prideful, them of the Small Vehicle's mind,
Monks who've broke their precepts, the false Arhats he will find,
And keep a distance from them, yeah, leave them far behind.

Nuns who like to joke and laugh, with a heart so light and free,
Those attached to five desires, seekin' extinction, wild and free,
And every single laywoman, he ain't to be close, you see.

But if they come with a good heart, to hear the Buddha's way,
That Bodhisattva, brave and bold, with nothing to repay,
He'll preach the Dharma to them, chase the darkness from the day.
Widows, virgins, those not fully men, he'll keep them at a bay,
No intimacy, no closeness, just the truth he'll gently say.

Butchers, slaughterers, hunters who kill for gain,
Fishers and the meat sellers, those who live in worldly pain,
Or those who sell a woman's beauty, yeah, from them he must refrain.
Wrestlers and the gamblers, with their games of chance and might,
And all the loose women, he'll keep 'em out of sight.

He won't be alone with no woman, in a place secluded and so deep,
To preach the sacred Dharma, secrets he must keep.
When he preaches, no jokin' laughter, from his lips it won't leap.
To a village for his alms, a fellow monk he'll take along,
If no one's there to join him, to the Buddha's name he'll strongly long.

This is the sphere of action, and proximity, it's true,
Dwellin' in these two spheres, peacefully the Dharma he'll pursue.
No superior, medium, inferior, no conditioned, no unreal,
No real, no unreal Dharmas, no man or woman, that's the deal.
No grasp of any phenomena, no knowledge, nothing he can feel.

This is the Bodhisattva's way, the sphere of action, understand,
All Dharmas are but empty, like the shifting desert sand.
No permanent existence, no arising, no decease,
This is the place of wisdom, where the wise can find their peace.

No upside-down contemplation, if phenomena exist or not,
No real or unreal somethin', no arising, that's his lot.
In a secluded place he'll sit, his mind he'll cultivate and bind,
Peaceful and unmoving, like Mount Sumeru, he'll always find.

All Dharmas are like empty space, no substance, it is known,
They ain't firm, they don't arise, no appearance has been shown.
Motionless and never retrogressin', in a single form they dwell,
This is called the sphere of proximity, a story it can tell.

If a monk, after my passing, enters these spheres so clear,
When he preaches this here sutra, he'll have no weakness, no fear.
A Bodhisattva, in a quiet room, with mindfulness so right,
Contemplates the Dharma's meaning, bathed in sacred light.
When he rises from his meditation, for kings and princes grand,
Ministers and citizens, and Brahmins across the land,
He'll reveal, convert, expound, this scripture in his hand.
His heart will be so peaceful, no fear, no weakness, understand.

Mañjuśrī, this is the Bodhisattva, in the first Dharma he'll reside,
And he'll preach the Lotus Sutra, with nothing left to hide,
In that future evil age, with wisdom as his guide.
"Furthermore, Mañjuśrī, after the Tathagata's passing, in the period of the final Dharma, if a person wishes to preach this sutra, he should dwell in the peaceful and joyful practice. When he preaches the Dharma or reads the sutra, he should not delight in speaking of the faults of others or of other scriptures. He should not be disrespectful to other Dharma teachers, or speak of the good and bad or the long and short of others. For the śrāvaka followers, he should not call them by name and speak of their faults, nor should he call them by name and praise their good qualities. He should also not give rise to a mind of resentment or dislike. By cultivating this kind of peaceful and joyful mind, those who listen will not oppose his wishes. If they have a difficult question, he should not answer with the Small Vehicle Dharma, but should only explain with the Great Vehicle, causing them to attain All-Knowing Wisdom."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
The Bodhisattva, with a peaceful soul,
Preaches the Dharma, makes the spirit whole.
On pure ground he takes his seat,
Washes off the dust, from head to feet.
New robes he wears, so clean and bright,
Pure inside and out, shining light.

He sits so easy, on his Dharma throne,
Answers all the questions, makes the truth be known.
Monks and nuns, lay folk too,
Kings and princes, me and you,
With gentle words and meaning deep,
Secrets of the Dharma, he will keep.

If hard questions come, he won't delay,
He'll answer with wisdom, light up the way.
With parables and stories, he makes it plain,
Explains the Dharma, through sun and rain.
These expedient means, a helping hand,
Awaken enlightenment, across the land.
They grow in spirit, day by day,
Walk that Buddha Way, come what may.

No lazy mind, no weary sigh,
He casts off worries, beneath the sky.
With a heart of compassion, strong and true,
He preaches the Dharma, for me and for you.
Day and night, the supreme Way he shows,
With countless stories, as the river flows.

He reveals it to all, makes their hearts rejoice,
Not seeking comfort, or a material choice.
Just one thought, deep in his soul,
"Preaching the Dharma, makes me whole,
I wish to attain the Buddha's light,
And lead all beings, out of the night."

This is the great benefit, pure and sweet,
A peaceful offering, complete.
After my passing, if a monk be found,
Who expounds this Lotus Sutra, on holy ground,
No jealousy or hatred in his heart,
Free from all afflictions, right from the start.

No worries vex him, no curses sting,
No fear of weapons, or what they bring.
He's not banished, through patience he stays,
A wise man like this, through all his days.
He skillfully cultivates his mind so clear,
Dwells in peaceful joy, year after year.
The merits of this person, so vast and wide,
Cannot be counted, no place to hide.
Millions of kalpas, they stretch on and on,
Beyond all telling, from dusk till dawn.
"Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Mañjuśrī, in the future, at the time of the final Dharma, when the Dharma is about to be extinguished, those who uphold, read, and recite this scripture should not have a mind of jealousy, flattery, or deceit. He should also not be disrespectful or abuse those who seek the Buddha Way, or look for their good and bad qualities. If they are monks, nuns, laymen, or laywomen, or those who seek the śrāvaka path, the pratyekabuddha path, or the Bodhisattva Way, he should not trouble them or cause them to have doubts and regrets. He should not say to them: 'You are still very far from the Way and will never be able to attain All-Knowing Wisdom. Why? Because you are lazy and slack in the Way.' He also should not engage in verbal games about the Dharma or in any disputes.
He should give rise to a mind of great compassion for all sentient beings. He should give rise to the thought of a compassionate father for all the Tathagatas. He should give rise to the thought of a great teacher for all the Bodhisattvas. For all the great Bodhisattvas of the ten directions, he should always, with a deep heart, bow in reverence. He should preach the Dharma equally to all sentient beings. In accordance with the Dharma, he should not preach too much or too little. Even for those who have a deep love for the Dharma, he should not preach too much for them."
"Mañjuśrī, if the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva in the future, at the time of the final Dharma, when the Dharma is about to be extinguished, is complete in this third peaceful and joyful practice, no one will be able to trouble or confuse him when he preaches this sutra. He will get good fellow students to read and recite this sutra together, and he will also get a great assembly to come and listen. When they have listened, they will be able to uphold it. Having upheld it, they will be able to recite it. Having recited it, they will be able to preach it. Having preached it, they will be able to copy it, or cause others to copy it, and make offerings to the sutra scrolls with reverence, respect, and praise."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If you wanna preach this sutra, hear my words, it's true,
Lay aside that jealousy, hatred, and arrogance too.
No flattery, no falsehood, just honest, upright ways,
Gotta walk that righteous path through all your livin' days.

Don't be lookin' down on nobody, don't play no verbal games,
'Bout the Dharma, keep it real, callin' folks by their true names.
Don't cause no doubt or regret, don't ever say with scorn,
"You ain't gonna be a Buddha," from the day that you were born.

The Buddha's son, when he preaches, gotta be gentle, soft, and slow,
Patient with all creation, lettin' his compassion flow.
No lazy mind, my brother, gotta keep that spirit bright,
Shinin' like a beacon in the darkest, deepest night.

Great Bodhisattvas from the ten directions, they practice for all man,
Out of pure compassion, holdin' out a helpin' hand.
Gotta show them due respect, they're teachers, wise and grand,
Preachin' that sweet Dharma across this troubled land.

For all the World-Honored Buddhas, think of 'em as a father so supreme,
Cast away that arrogant mind, just live within the dream.
Preach that Dharma freely, let the truth ring out and soar,
No hindrance, no obstruction, knockin' on every door.

This third Dharma, it's the way, the wise ones understand,
Guard it with a single mind, across this weary land.
Practice peaceful, joyful conduct, till your time on earth is through,
And immeasurable beings, they gonna reverence you.
"Furthermore, Mañjuśrī, if the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva, in the future, at the time of the final Dharma, when the Dharma is about to be extinguished, upholds this Lotus Sutra, he should give rise to a mind of great compassion for the laypeople and monastics. For those who are not Bodhisattvas, he should give rise to a mind of great pity. He should think: 'Such people are at a great loss. The Tathagata used expedient means to preach the Dharma according to what was appropriate, but they have not heard it, known it, awakened to it, asked about it, believed in it, or understood it. Even though they do not ask about, believe in, or understand this sutra, when I attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi, I will, wherever they are, use the power of my supernatural abilities and wisdom to guide them and cause them to dwell in this Dharma.'
Mañjuśrī, the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva who is complete in this fourth Dharma after the Tathagata’s passing, will have no faults when he preaches this Dharma. He will always be respected and worshiped by the monks, nuns, laymen, laywomen, kings, princes, ministers, citizens, Brahmins, and elders. The heavenly beings in the sky, for the sake of hearing the Dharma, will also constantly follow him. If he is in a village, city, or secluded forest, and someone comes to ask him a difficult question, the heavenly beings, day and night, will protect him for the sake of the Dharma, and will cause all who listen to rejoice. Why? Because this sutra is protected by the supernatural power of all the Buddhas of the past, present, and future.
Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra is so rare that in immeasurable lands, even its name is not heard, let alone being able to see, uphold, read, or recite it."
The Parable of the King’s Jewel
"Mañjuśrī, it is like a powerful wheel-turning holy king who, with his majestic power, wishes to conquer many lands. But the small kings do not obey his command. The wheel-turning king then raises various armies and goes to fight and punish them. The king, seeing his soldiers who have performed great feats in battle, is filled with great joy and rewards them according to their merits. He may give them land and houses, villages and cities, or robes and personal adornments, or various precious treasures, gold, silver, lapis lazuli, tridacna, agate, coral, amber, elephants, horses, carriages, slaves, and people. But the one jewel that is in his topknot, he does not give away. Why? Only the king has this one jewel on his head. If he were to give it away, his relatives would be greatly surprised and alarmed."
"Mañjuśrī, the Tathagata is also like this. With the power of meditation and wisdom, he has attained the land of the Dharma and reigns over the three realms. But all the demon kings do not obey him. The holy and virtuous generals of the Tathagata fight with them—with the demon of the five aggregates, the demon of afflictions, and the demon of death. Those who perform great feats, who extinguish the three poisons, leave the three realms, and break the nets of Māra, the Tathagata is also filled with great joy. The Lotus Sutra is the one Dharma that can cause sentient beings to attain All-Knowing Wisdom. The world is filled with many who resent it and find it difficult to believe. It is a Dharma that was not spoken of before, but now it is being spoken."
"Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra is the first of all the Tathagata's sermons. Among all the preachings, it is the most profound and subtle. He gives it away at the very end, just like that powerful king who protected the luminous jewel for a long time and only now gives it away. Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra is the secret treasury of all the Tathagatas. Among all the sutras, it is the most supreme. He has protected it for a long night of existence and has not preached it casually, but only today, he is revealing it for you."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Now listen here, in the future, when the times get rough,
The ones who hold this sutra, they'll show their loving stuff.
Lay or monastic, or just a soul so free,
Gotta find compassion, for all the world to see.

Now some folks ain't heard it, some just don't believe,
But they're at a great loss, my heart it does grieve.
When I find the Buddha Way, with my expedient means so grand,
I'll preach this here Dharma, across the whole wide land.

Like a Wheel-turning king, with his soldiers brave and bold,
Rewards them with treasures, a story to be told.
Elephants and horses, carriages so fine,
Adornments for their bodies, and lands that truly shine.

Houses and villages, cities by the score,
Robes and precious jewels, and so much more.
Slaves and all their riches, given with a joyful hand,
A mighty good feeling, across the whole wide land.

But if there's a brave soldier, who does a mighty deed,
The king takes his topknot jewel, plantin' a royal seed.
The Tathagata's like that, the King of all that's true,
With mighty patience, and wisdom shinin' through.

With great compassion, he teaches the world his way,
Sees all human suffering, from night until the day.
They're fightin' all the demons, seekin' to be free,
For these weary souls, he preaches eternally.

With great expedient means, he preaches these sutras deep,
When folks gain their power, secrets he will keep.
Then finally he preaches, this Lotus Sutra bright,
Like the king's topknot jewel, shinin' in the light.

This sutra's the most revered, the highest of them all,
I've always protected it, ready for your call.
Today's the right moment, for me to let it fly,
After my departure, if you seek the Buddha high.

Want to preach this sutra, with peace and joy to find,
Gotta stick to these four Dharmas, and leave all worries behind.
Read this sacred sutra, no worries, no despair,
No sickness in your body, just a complexion fresh and fair.

No poverty or low status, no ugly face you'll see,
Folks will love to see you, like a sage they long to be.
The sons of all the gods, they'll serve you night and day,
Knives and sticks won't harm you, poisons can't hold sway.

If someone dares abuse you, their mouth will then be blocked,
You'll travel without fear, like a lion, strong and rocked.
Your wisdom light will shine, like the sun up in the sky,
If you dream, it's wondrous visions, passin' by.

See all the Tathagatas, on a lion's seat so grand,
Monks gathered all around, listenin' to understand.
Dragons and the spirits, asuras in their might,
Numerous as Ganges sands, joinin' palms in sacred light.

See your own body preachin', the Dharma for them all,
Buddhas with golden bodies, answerin' every call.
Emmitting boundless light, shinin' everywhere,
With Brahma's voice they teach, with wisdom beyond compare.

Buddha preaches Dharma, for all four groups to hear,
See yourself in the assembly, holdin' hands so dear.
Hear the Dharma with joy, offerings you will make,
Attain dhāranīs of wisdom, for goodness gracious sake.

Buddha knows your heart, deep in the Buddha Way,
Gives you a prophecy, for enlightenment that day.
"You, noble soul, in a life that's yet to be,
Will gain boundless wisdom, and the Buddha Way so free.

Your land will be adorned, so pure and grand and vast,
Four groups will gather 'round, their devotion built to last."
See yourself in the forest, cultivatin' good and true,
Realizin' all true natures, for me and for you.

Enter deep meditation, see Buddhas far and wide,
Golden bodies shinin', with hundred marks inside.
Hear the Dharma, preach it, always good dreams you'll find,
Dream you are a king, leavin' your palace behind.

Give up all your desires, walk to enlighten's shore,
Under the bodhi tree, and ask for nothing more.
For seven days you seek the Way, with Buddhas' wisdom bright,
Then you rise and turn the wheel, shinin' in the light.

Preach the Dharma for ages, for millions of kalpas long,
Subtle, unconditioned Dharma, where all souls belong.
Then you'll enter nirvana, like a lamp when smoke is gone,
If in evil ages, you preach this sacred dawn.

This person gains great benefit, all the merits I have shown,
A bluesy kind of blessing, right here in your home.



Chapter 15: Welling Up from the Earth
At that time, the Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas who had come from other lands, numbering more than eight times the sands of the Ganges, rose from the assembly, joined their palms in reverence, and said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, if you would permit us, after the Buddha's passing, we will diligently and with great effort, protect, uphold, read, recite, copy, and make offerings to this scripture in this Saha world. We will then widely preach it in this land."
At that time, the Buddha said to the assembly of Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas: "Stop, noble men. There is no need for you to protect this sutra. Why? In my Saha world, there are already Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the sands of sixty thousand Ganges Rivers. Each of these Bodhisattvas has a retinue of as many sentient beings as the sands of sixty thousand Ganges Rivers. These people are able, after my passing, to protect, uphold, read, recite, and widely preach this sutra."
When the Buddha said this, the ground of the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world split open and trembled. From within, countless millions of millions of Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas rose all at once. These Bodhisattvas all had golden bodies, were adorned with the thirty-two marks, and radiated immeasurable light. They had all been living below the Saha world in the empty space of this realm. These Bodhisattvas heard the voice of Śākyamuni Buddha and came forth from below.
Each of these Bodhisattvas was the leader of a great assembly and had a retinue of sentient beings as numerous as the sands of sixty thousand Ganges Rivers. How much more so for those who had retinues of fifty thousand, forty thousand, thirty thousand, twenty thousand, or ten thousand Ganges River sands?
