How much more still for those with even one Ganges River sand, a half, a quarter, or even one part of ten million nayuta? How much more for those with a retinue of millions and millions, or thousands and thousands, or hundreds, or tens, or even five, four, three, two, or one disciple? How much more for those who were alone and delighted in the practice of solitude? Such Bodhisattvas were immeasurable and boundless, beyond the reach of calculation and parables.
When these Bodhisattvas had emerged from the earth, they each went into the air to the wonderful seven-jeweled stupa, to the place of Abundant Treasures Tathagata and Śākyamuni Buddha. When they arrived, they bowed their heads at the feet of the two World-Honored Ones, and also paid reverence to the Buddhas on the lion’s seats under all the jeweled trees. They circled the Buddhas three times, joined their palms in reverence, and praised them with the various methods of Bodhisattva praise. They stood to one side, joyfully looking up at the two World-Honored Ones.
From the moment these Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas first emerged, they praised the Buddhas with various methods of Bodhisattva praise. In this way, a period of fifty minor kalpas passed. At that time, Śākyamuni Buddha sat in silence, and the four groups of the assembly also sat in silence for fifty minor kalpas. But by the supernatural power of the Buddha, the great assembly felt that it had been only a half day. At that time, the four groups of the assembly, also by the supernatural power of the Buddha, saw these Bodhisattvas filling the air of immeasurable hundreds of thousands of millions of worlds.
Among this assembly of Bodhisattvas, there were four leaders: first, Superior Conduct; second, Limitless Conduct; third, Pure Conduct; and fourth, Firmly-Established Conduct. These four Bodhisattvas were the foremost guides of the assembly. In front of the great assembly, they each joined their palms, looked at Śākyamuni Buddha, and asked him about his well-being: "World-Honored One, are you in good health and without much sickness? Are you at ease in your practice? Are those who should be saved easy to convert? Have they not caused the World-Honored One to become weary?"
At that time, the four great Bodhisattvas spoke in verse:
World-Honored One, tell me true, are you at ease?
In good health, without much sickness, if you please?
When you're converting souls, do you not feel the strain,
Or do they wear you out again and again?
And all these folks you're tryin' to save, are they easy to sway?
Or do they try your patience, every single day?
Don't they cause the World-Honored One, to sigh and moan,
Leaving you feeling weary, down to the bone?
At that time, the World-Honored One spoke to the great assembly of Bodhisattvas, saying: "It is so, it is so, noble men. The Tathagata is at ease, in good health and without much sickness. All sentient beings are easy to convert, and they do not cause me to become weary. Why? These sentient beings, from lifetime to lifetime, have always received my conversion. They have also, in the presence of past Buddhas, shown reverence and respect and planted all kinds of roots of goodness. When these sentient beings first see my body and hear what I preach, they immediately believe it and enter the wisdom of the Tathagata. For those who have first cultivated the Small Vehicle, I now also cause them to hear this sutra and enter the Buddha's wisdom."
At that time, all the great Bodhisattvas spoke in verse:
Oh, excellent, excellent, it's true,
Great, valiant World-Honored One, through and through.
All these sentient beings, they ain't hard to sway,
Gonna hear that wisdom, come what may.
You preach the Buddha's deep wisdom, a profound, sweet sound,
We rejoice when they hear it, on hallowed ground.
They believe and they practice, a new path they find,
Leaving all that old trouble, far behind.
At that time, the World-Honored One praised the four great Bodhisattvas: "Excellent, excellent, noble men! You are able to give rise to a mind of joy for the Tathagata."
At that time, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya and his assembly of Bodhisattvas as numerous as the sands of eight thousand Ganges Rivers, all thought: "From the past until now, we have never seen or heard of such a great assembly of Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas rising from the earth, dwelling before the World-Honored One, joining their palms, making offerings, and asking about the Tathagata’s well-being."
At that time, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya, knowing the thoughts in the hearts of the Bodhisattvas, as numerous as the sands of eight thousand Ganges Rivers, and wishing to resolve his own doubts, joined his palms toward the Buddha and spoke in verse:
Countless Bodhisattvas, a sight to behold,
Never seen such a gathering, stories untold.
We beg of the Honored One, with two feet on the ground,
Tell us, where did they come from, and why are they around?  
With giant bodies and power, supernatural and grand,
Wisdom flows like a river, across the whole wide land.
Their resolve is solid, patience strong and deep,
A joy for all beings, secrets they keep.
Where did they come from, this magnificent crew?
Each Bodhisattva, with a retinue so vast,
Like sands of the Ganges, their numbers surely last.
Some great Bodhisattvas, with sixty thousand strong,
Seeking the Buddha Way, singing a single song.
These teachers so mighty, with their sixty thousand crew,
Came to make offerings, and see this sutra through.
Fifty thousand, forty thousand, thirty, twenty, and ten,
A thousand, a hundred, the numbers just extend.
One Ganges sand, a half, a quarter, a millionth part,
Ten thousand nayuta, right from the very start. 
Millions of millions of disciples, half a million too,
Their numbers just keep growing, for me and for you.
From a million down to ten thousand, then a thousand, a hundred,
Fifty, then just ten, numbers still unfettered.
Even three, two, or one, alone without a band,
Delighting in their solitude, across this weary land.
All have come to the Buddha's place, more than I can say.
If a person tried to count, through kalpas long and deep,
Like sands of the Ganges, secrets they would keep.
These Bodhisattvas, with majesty and grace,
Who preached the Dharma to them, in time and in space?
Who converted and completed, their souls so bright and keen?
From whom did they first rise, with a mind so pure and clean?
Whose Buddha Dharma did they praise, with voices loud and clear?
Whose sutras did they uphold, banishing all fear?
What Buddha Way did they cultivate, under the moon and sun?
These Bodhisattvas, with power and wisdom's might,
Caused the earth to tremble, in the fading light.
From within the ground they sprung, a sight I've never known,
World-Honored One, please tell me, where have they truly flown?
And the names of their lands, tell me if you please.
I've traveled to all lands, through the sky and through the trees,
But never seen this assembly, not a single face I know.
Suddenly from the earth they came, putting on a show.
World-Honored One, please tell me, the reason why they're here.
This great assembly now, countless, holding back no fear.
All these Bodhisattvas, want to know the truth,
The causes and conditions, from beginning to our youth.
Oh World-Honored One, with virtue beyond compare,
Please resolve our doubts, hanging in the air.
At that time, the emanations of Śākyamuni Buddha who had come from countless millions of millions of lands in other directions, were sitting on the lion’s seats under the jeweled trees in the eight directions. Their attendants each saw this great multitude of Bodhisattvas emerging from the ground in all four directions of the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world and dwelling in the air. They each said to their Buddha: "World-Honored One, from where has this immeasurable, boundless, and inconceivable assembly of Bodhisattvas come?"
At that time, all the Buddhas each said to their attendants: "Noble men, just wait for a moment. There is a Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva named Maitreya, who has been given a prophecy by Śākyamuni Buddha to be the next Buddha. He has already asked this question. The Buddha will now answer it. You will all be able to hear it on your own."
At that time, Śākyamuni Buddha said to Bodhisattva Maitreya: "Excellent, excellent, Ajita! You have been able to ask the Buddha such a great matter. You should all be of one mind, put on the armor of diligence, and give rise to a firm resolve. The Tathagata now wishes to reveal and declare the wisdom of all Buddhas, the independent supernatural power of all Buddhas, the lion's roar power of all Buddhas, and the powerful and majestic power of all Buddhas."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Be diligent, be of one mind, y'all hear what I say,
'Bout to speak on this matter, don't let no doubt hold sway.
Buddha's wisdom's a mystery, deep as the ocean blue,
So open up your hearts, let that good faith shine through.

Gotta find that power of faith, let it be your guide,
Dwell in goodness and patience, with nothing left to hide.
This Dharma you ain't heard before, gonna lay it on the line,
Now every single one of you, gonna hear it, it's your time.

I'm here to comfort you, now don't you fret or fear,
The Buddha speaks no false words, his wisdom's always clear.
Immeasurable and endless, like a river ever flowing,
So just listen closely now, let that understanding keep growing.

This supreme Dharma I found, it's subtle and it's deep,
Hard to tell it apart, secrets it does keep.
But I'm gonna speak it now, with a soulful, steady hand,
So listen with a single mind, across this troubled land.


At that time, the World-Honored One, having spoken this verse, said to Bodhisattva Maitreya: "I now declare to this great assembly and to you: Ajita, these great Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas, immeasurable, countless, and asaṃkhyeya, who have sprung forth from the earth, whom you have never seen before, I, in this Saha world, since attaining Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi, have converted, guided, and shown them the Way. I have subdued their minds and caused them to give rise to the mind for the Way. These Bodhisattvas all dwell in the empty space below this Saha world, and they read, recite, and master all the scriptures. They contemplate, differentiate, and remember them properly. Ajita, these noble men do not delight in being in a great assembly or in speaking much. They always delight in quiet places and diligently practice with vigor without ever resting. They do not rely on gods or humans to live. They always delight in profound wisdom and have no hindrances. They always delight in the Dharma of the Buddhas, diligently seeking supreme wisdom with a single mind."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
I, since a long time ago, Have converted these assemblies.
Ajita, listen close now, you oughta know,
These great Bodhisattvas, since long, long ago,
Through countless kalpas, they been workin' it out,
Cultivatin' Buddha's wisdom, no shadow of doubt.

Yeah, I converted 'em all, every single one,
Caused 'em to rise up, with the great mind for the Sun.
These are my children, they walkin' this earth,
Always practicin' hard, showin' their true worth.

Their spirits find comfort in a quiet, lonely place,
Left the noisy crowds behind, ain't no rush, no race.
Don't delight in much talkin', they got wisdom to find,
Leavin' the chatter behind 'em, settin' their soul free, mind by mind.

Such children, they learnin' the Dharma, my sacred song,
Progressin' day and night, where they truly belong.
Seekin' the Buddha Way, with a tireless, steady beat,
Movin' on down the road, on their weary feet.

They dwell in the empty space, beneath this Saha world,
Their resolve and their power, strong, they never unfurled.
Constantly seekin' wisdom, a light in the dark night,
Shinin' like a beacon, burnin' ever so bright.

They preach the wonderful Dharmas, with a fearless, joyful sound,
No fear in their hearts, when the truth is all around.
I was in Gaya city, under that bodhi tree,
Found the true enlightenment, for all the world to see.

Turned the supreme Dharma wheel, for the lost and the found,
That's when I converted 'em, from the cold, hard ground.
Caused 'em to first raise their mind, for the path and the light,
Now they're in a steady state, shinin' ever so bright.

They'll all attain Buddhahood, it's the truth I declare,
You gotta believe me, with a single mind and a prayer.
I, since a long, long time ago, with a love so deep and wide,
Converted these assemblies, stayin' right by their side.
At that time, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya and the countless Bodhisattvas felt doubt in their hearts. They were astonished by this unprecedented event and thought: "How could the World-Honored One, in such a short time, convert such immeasurable and countless asaṃkhyeya great Bodhisattvas and cause them to dwell in Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi?" He then said to the Buddha:
"World-Honored One, when you were a prince, you left the palace of the Śākyas. Not far from the city of Gaya, you sat at the place of enlightenment and attained Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. Since then, only a little over forty years have passed. World-Honored One, how, in such a short time, have you been able to do such a great work of a Buddha and, with the Buddha's power and merits, convert such an immeasurable assembly of great Bodhisattvas, causing them to attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi?"
"World-Honored One, even if a person were to count these great Bodhisattva assemblies for millions of kalpas, they could not exhaust or reach the end of their number. These beings, since ancient times, under immeasurable and countless Buddhas, have planted all kinds of good roots, completed the Bodhisattva Way, and constantly cultivated pure conduct. World-Honored One, this matter is difficult for the world to believe. It is like a person who is handsome and has black hair, and is only twenty-five years old. He points to a hundred-year-old man and says, 'This is my son.' And the hundred-year-old man also points to the young man and says, 'This is my father, who fathered us.' This is a matter that the entire world would find difficult to believe."
"The Buddha is also like this. The time since you attained the Way is truly not long, and yet this great assembly of Bodhisattvas has, for immeasurable millions of kalpas, diligently practiced for the sake of the Buddha Way. They have skillfully entered, dwelt in, and come out of immeasurable hundreds of thousands of millions of samadhis, attained great supernatural powers, cultivated pure conduct for a long time, and are skilled in gradually learning all wholesome Dharmas. They are eloquent in asking and answering questions, they are the treasures of the world, and are extremely rare in all the worlds. Yet today, the World-Honored One says that when he attained the Buddha Way, he first caused them to give rise to the mind of enlightenment, and he converted and guided them, causing them to aspire to Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. The World-Honored One attained Buddhahood not long ago, and yet you were able to do this great and meritorious deed.
Although we believe what the Buddha says, that his words are spoken with expedient means and are never false, and that the Buddha knows and has a thorough understanding of everything, if the new Bodhisattvas, after the Buddha’s passing, were to hear these words, they might not believe them and thus commit the sin of rejecting the Dharma. For this reason, World-Honored One, we ask that you explain this matter to remove our doubts, so that in the future, when noble men hear this, they will also not give rise to doubt."
At that time, Bodhisattva Maitreya, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Lord Buddha left his clan, it's true,
By Gaya's city, 'neath the Bodhi, too.
It wasn't long ago, that much is clear,
But his many sons, they conquer fear.

These countless sons, they walk the Way,
With powers mystic, come what may.
Like lotuses pure, from mud they rise,
Undefiled by the world, beneath the skies.

With hearts so humble, they stand tall,
Before the World-Honored, hearing his call.
This mystery deep, it's hard to see,
How can such wonders truly be?

The Buddha's Way, so newly found,
Yet such achievements on holy ground.
Please clear our doubts, and make it plain,
Unveil the truth, again and again.

Like a young man, twenty-five years old,
Shows a son of a hundred, with stories untold.
White hair and wrinkles, a weathered face,
"I fathered these," he says with grace.

The son replies, "My father, you see,
He raised me up, for all to agree."
A young father, old son, a curious sight,
The world would question, with all its might.

The World-Honored, he's like this too,
His time on the Way, so fresh and new.
But these Bodhisattvas, with spirits bold,
Have wisdom and courage, worth more than gold.

For countless ages, they've walked the path,
Skilled in the Dharma, escaping its wrath.
Their hearts are fearless, their answers keen,
The Bodhisattva Way, a glorious scene.

Their patience holds strong, their forms so grand,
Praised by the Buddhas, across every land.
They preach the Dharma, with skillful art,
And keep to themselves, deep in the heart.

No joy in crowds, but meditation's call,
They seek the Buddha Way, to give their all.
In empty space below, they make their home,
Till enlightenment's light, helps them to roam.

We've heard the Buddha, and doubt we have none,
But for the future, 'til rising of sun.
Explain it for them, to bring light and grace,
Enlightenment's journey, in every place.

If this sutra brings doubt, and they turn away,
To evil destinies, they'll fall astray.
So please explain now, the how and the why,
These countless Bodhisattvas, reaching for the sky.

In such a short time, they've found their mind's quest,
On non-retrogression's ground, they now will rest.
How did it happen, this change so profound?
Let wisdom and truth, forever resound.


Chapter 16: The Lifespan of the Tathagata
At that time, the Buddha said to all the Bodhisattvas and the entire great assembly: "Noble men, you must believe and understand the Tathagata's honest and truthful words." He again said to the great assembly: "You must believe and understand the Tathagata's honest and truthful words." And again, he said to the great assembly: "You must believe and understand the Tathagata's honest and truthful words."
At that time, the assembly of Bodhisattvas, led by Maitreya, joined their palms and said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, we only wish that you would speak. We will believe and accept the Buddha's words." After they made this request three times, they said again: "We only wish that you would speak. We will believe and accept the Buddha's words."
At that time, the World-Honored One, knowing that the Bodhisattvas' three requests would not stop, said to them: "You must listen carefully to the secret and supernatural power of the Tathagata. All the worlds of gods, humans, and asuras believe that Śākyamuni Buddha left the palace of the Śākya clan, and not far from the city of Gaya, sat at the place of enlightenment and attained Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi. But noble men, I have, in fact, attained Buddhahood since immeasurable, boundless, hundreds of thousands of millions of nayuta asaṃkhyeya kalpas ago."
"It is like five hundred million million nayuta asaṃkhyeya three-thousand-great-thousand-fold worlds. If there was a person who ground them into fine dust, and then, after passing five hundred million million nayuta asaṃkhyeya worlds to the east, he dropped one speck of dust. In this way, he continues eastward until all the fine dust is used up. Noble men, what do you think? Can these worlds be calculated or can their number be known by a thought?"
Bodhisattva Maitreya and the others all said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, these worlds are immeasurable and boundless. They are not known by calculation, nor can they be reached by the power of the mind. All the śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas, with their pure wisdom, cannot contemplate or know their number. We, who dwell on the ground of non-retrogression, also cannot reach this. World-Honored One, such worlds are truly immeasurable and boundless."
At that time, the Buddha said to the great assembly of Bodhisattvas: "Noble men, I will now clearly declare this for you. All these worlds, whether a speck of dust was dropped on them or not, if they were all made into dust, and each speck of dust were a kalpa, the time since I attained Buddhahood would be more than that by hundreds of thousands of millions of nayuta asaṃkhyeya kalpas. Since that time, I have constantly been in this Saha world, preaching the Dharma and converting beings. I have also, in other places, in hundreds of thousands of millions of nayuta asaṃkhyeya worlds, guided and benefited sentient beings."
"Noble men, in this interim, I have spoken of Buddhas such as Burning Lamp and have also said that they entered nirvana. All of this was differentiated through expedient means. Noble men, if there are sentient beings who come to me, I use my Buddha eye to observe their faith and their sharpness or dullness of faculties. According to who needs to be saved, I manifest myself in different places, with different names and ages. I also declare that I am about to enter nirvana, and I use all kinds of expedient means to preach the subtle and wonderful Dharma, which can cause sentient beings to give rise to a joyful heart. Noble men, when the Tathagata sees sentient beings who delight in the small Dharma and have little virtue and heavy defilements, he preaches for these people, saying: 'I renounced my home when I was young and attained Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi.' However, I have actually attained Buddhahood for a long, long time, but for the sake of converting sentient beings and causing them to enter the Buddha Way, I speak in this way."
"Noble men, all the scriptures expounded by the Tathagata are for the sake of saving and liberating sentient beings. Sometimes he speaks of his own body, and sometimes he speaks of other bodies. Sometimes he shows his own deeds, and sometimes he shows the deeds of others. All that he says is truthful and not false. Why? The Tathagata truly knows and sees the nature of the three realms. There is no such thing as life and death, or leaving and returning. There is also no such thing as dwelling in the world or passing away. It is neither real nor false, neither the same nor different. It is not like the three realms, which are seen as the three realms. Such matters are clearly seen by the Tathagata, without any error. Because sentient beings have various natures, various desires, various practices, and various thoughts and differentiations, wishing to cause them to give rise to roots of goodness, I use various causes, parables, and words to preach the Dharma in various ways. The work of a Buddha has never ceased, not even for a moment. In this way, the time since I attained Buddhahood has been very long. My life span is immeasurable asaṃkhyeya kalpas, and I constantly dwell in the world and do not pass away."
"Noble men, the life span I attained through my practice of the Bodhisattva Way is still not exhausted, and it is more than double the number I gave above. However, I am not truly entering extinction, but I declare that I am about to attain extinction. The Tathagata uses this expedient means to convert sentient beings. Why? If the Buddha were to dwell in the world for a long time, people with little virtue would not plant roots of goodness. They would be poor and of low status, and would be attached to the five desires, falling into a net of thoughts and false views. If they saw that the Tathagata always exists and does not pass away, they would become arrogant and lazy. They would not give rise to the thought that he is difficult to encounter or a respectful mind. For this reason, the Tathagata, using expedient means, says: 'Monks, you should know that the appearance of the Buddhas in the world is difficult to encounter.' Why? For those with little virtue, after countless millions of kalpas, they may see a Buddha, or they may not. Because of this, I say: 'Monks, the Tathagata is difficult to see.' When these sentient beings hear such words, they will surely give rise to the thought that he is difficult to encounter, and their hearts will be filled with longing and thirst for the Buddha. They will then plant roots of goodness. For this reason, even though the Tathagata does not truly pass away, he says he is entering extinction."
"Furthermore, noble men, the Dharmas of all the Tathagatas are all like this. For the sake of saving sentient beings, their words are all true and not false.
The Parable of the Good Doctor
"Imagine a good doctor who is intelligent and wise, and who is well-versed in prescriptions and medicines, and skilled at curing all kinds of sicknesses. This man has many children, perhaps ten, twenty, or even a hundred. Because of a business matter, he goes far away to another country. Later, his children drink poison that was given to them by someone else. When the poison takes effect, they become confused and lie on the ground, rolling in agony.
At that time, their father returns home. Some of the children who drank the poison have lost their original minds, and some have not. When they see their father from a distance, they are all filled with great joy. They bow down and ask about his safe and peaceful return: 'We were foolish and accidentally drank poison. We wish that you would save and cure us and grant us a longer life.' The father sees his children suffering like this. He follows the prescriptions in the scriptures, finds good medicinal herbs, and has them prepared with all the right colors, fragrances, and tastes. He grinds and sifts them and gives them to his children to take, saying: 'This is a great, good medicine. Its color and fragrance are both wonderful, and it is complete with all good tastes. You should all take it. It will quickly remove your suffering and afflictions, and you will have no more sickness.'
"Among the children, those who had not lost their minds saw that this good medicine had a wonderful color and fragrance, and they immediately took it. Their sickness was completely cured. The others, who had lost their minds, although they saw their father return and were happy to ask about his well-being and seek a cure for their sickness, refused to take the medicine when it was given to them. Why? Because the poison had gone deep and they had lost their original minds. They thought that this medicine, with its wonderful color and fragrance, was not good.
The father thought: 'These children are pitiful. They have been poisoned, and their minds are all upside-down. Although they see me and are happy, and they seek to be saved and cured, they will not take this wonderful medicine. I must now devise an expedient means to cause them to take this medicine.' He then said: 'You should all know that I am now old, and my time to die has come. This good, great medicine will be left here. You can take it and take it. Do not worry that you will not be cured.'
Having given this instruction, he went to another country. He sent a messenger back to his home to say: 'Your father has died.' When the children heard of their father's passing, their hearts were filled with great sorrow and affliction. They thought: 'If our father were still alive, he would have compassion for us and be able to save and protect us. But now he has left us and passed away in a distant land.' They felt orphaned and alone, with no one to rely on. A constant feeling of grief filled their hearts, and their minds gradually became clear. They then realized that the medicine was good in its color, taste, and fragrance. They took it immediately, and the sickness from the poison was all cured. Their father heard that his children had all recovered, so he returned home and let them all see him."
"Noble men, what do you think? Is there anyone who could say that this good doctor committed a crime of falsehood?"
"No, World-Honored One."
The Buddha said: "I am also like this. The time since I attained Buddhahood has been an immeasurable, boundless, hundreds of thousands of millions of nayuta asaṃkhyeya kalpas. For the sake of sentient beings, I use the power of expedient means to say that I am about to enter extinction. There is no one who can, according to the Dharma, accuse me of a fault of falsehood."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Since I found my true Buddha way, many eons have flown by,
Countless millions, I've guided on their path beneath the sky.
I preach the Dharma, clear and strong, for souls lost in the night,
To bring 'em to the Buddha's light, and make their burdens light.

I use my means, a gentle trick, to show 'em I have gone,
But I'm right here, preachin' on, from dusk 'til early morn.
Though they don't see me, close at hand, with eyes that still are blind,
My power keeps me ever near, for those with open mind.

When they believe I've passed away, they bring their offerings true,
Their hearts are filled with longing deep, for a glimpse of me and you.
When faithful hearts turn soft and pure, no worldly goods they crave,
They give their bodies, lives and all, for the truth I came to save.

Then me and all my holy crew, on Vulture Peak appear,
I tell 'em then, "I'm always here, banish all your fear."
I use my means, a spirit strong, to show I'm here and gone,
For respectful souls in distant lands, who sing my joyful song.

I preach the Dharma, high and deep, though you don't hear my call,
You just believe I've passed away, standin' tall through it all.
When I see suffering in their eyes, and sorrow in their soul,
I hide away, to make 'em yearn, and make their spirits whole.

When longing fills their weary hearts, I show my face again,
My mystic power, like a river, flows without end.
On Vulture Peak, I make my home, and other places too,
When kalpas end, and fires rage, my land remains for you.

My land is peaceful, sweet and calm, with angels all around,
Gardens bright and halls so grand, where treasures can be found.
Jeweled trees with flowers bloom, for happy souls to play,
Heaven's drums keep beat and time, from night 'til break of day.

Mandara flowers, soft and light, on Buddha and the crowd,
My pure land ain't destroyed, my friend, though they see a fiery cloud.
Worries, fears, and troubles deep, these sinful souls they bear,
Because of their own wicked ways, and burdens hard to share.

For endless kalpas, they don't hear, the Jewels, a sacred sound,
But those who walk a righteous path, with gentleness profound,
They see my body, right and true, preachin' the Dharma's word,
Sometimes I say my life is long, a truth they've never heard.

For those who find me after long, I say I'm hard to see,
My wisdom light shines, far and wide, for all eternity.
My life, it spans a million years, from countless deeds I've done,
So wise ones, cast your doubts away, beneath the risin' sun.

For Buddha's words are never false, a truth that rings so clear,
Like a good doctor, with his tricks, to calm his children's fear.
He says he's gone, though he's alive, no lie can touch his name,
I am the father of this world, quenching suffering's flame.

For foolish souls, with minds astray, I say I've passed away,
'Cause when they see me all the time, they grow so proud each day.
Lazy, bound by worldly things, to evil paths they fall,
But I know who walks the righteous path, and who will heed my call.

I preach the Dharma, in its forms, for every soul in need,
I always think, "How can I sow, that wisdom's precious seed?"
To lead 'em to the highest truth, and swiftly, on that day,
To help 'em find the Buddha's form, and walk the righteous way.






Chapter 17: Merits of Distinction
At that time, the great assembly, having heard the Buddha speak of his life span as being so long and far-reaching, found that immeasurable, boundless, and asaṃkhyeya sentient beings gained great benefits. Then the World-Honored One said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya:
"Ajita, when I preached that the Tathagata's life span is long and far-reaching, sentient beings, as numerous as the sands of six hundred and eighty million million nayuta Ganges Rivers, attained the patience of non-arising. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas, one thousand times that number, attained the Dharma-gate of total recall (dhāranī). Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of one world attained the unhindered eloquence of joyful speech. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of one world attained the total recall of the whirling Dharma, with its hundreds of thousands of millions of varieties. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of a three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world were able to turn the non-retrogressing Dharma wheel. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of two thousand middle worlds were able to turn the pure Dharma wheel. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of a thousand small worlds will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi in eight lifetimes. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of four four-continents will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi in four lifetimes. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of three four-continents will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi in three lifetimes. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of two four-continents will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi in two lifetimes. Furthermore, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas as numerous as the dust motes of one four-continent will attain Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi in one lifetime. Furthermore, sentient beings as numerous as the dust motes of eight worlds all gave rise to the mind for Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
"When the Buddha spoke of these Bodhisattva-Mahāsattvas gaining the great benefits of the Dharma, heavenly maṇḍārava and mahāmaṇḍārava flowers rained down from the air. They were scattered upon the Buddhas sitting on the lion's seats under the immeasurable hundreds of thousands of millions of jeweled trees, and also upon Śākyamuni Buddha and the long-passed-away Abundant Treasures Tathagata in the seven-jeweled stupa. They were also scattered upon all the great Bodhisattvas and the four groups of the assembly.
"Fine sandalwood and incense like agarwood also rained down from the air. Heavenly drums sounded by themselves, with a subtle and profound sound. A thousand kinds of heavenly robes also rained down, along with garlands of pearls, garlands of mani jewels, and garlands of wish-fulfilling jewels. They filled the nine directions. Jeweled incense burners of many kinds, burning priceless incense, naturally spread everywhere as offerings to the great assembly. Above each Buddha, there were Bodhisattvas holding banners and canopies, moving in order, all the way up to the Brahma heavens. These Bodhisattvas, with their wonderful voices, sang countless verses, praising all the Buddhas."
At that time, Bodhisattva Maitreya rose from his seat, bared his right shoulder, joined his palms toward the Buddha, and spoke in verse:
The Buddha done preached a Dharma so rare,
A truth we ain't never heard, beyond compare.
The World-Honored One, with power so deep,
His life span, Lord, a secret he does keep.

His countless sons, they hear his voice so sweet,
Of Dharma's merits, joy from head to feet.
Some stand on ground where they can't fall behind,
Some grab that dhāranī, to ease their troubled mind.

Some speak with joyful words, like a river's flow,
Or recall a million whirlings, watchin' memories grow.
Bodhisattvas, like dust motes in the air,
Turn the Dharma wheel, beyond all care.

From a great-thousand-fold world, they rise and shine,
Turnin' that wheel, a cosmic, righteous design.
Then from a middle-thousand, they do appear,
Turnin' the pure Dharma wheel, banishin' all fear.

From a small-thousand-fold world, they come along,
Eight more lifetimes, Lord, then they'll sing their Buddha song.
And from four, three, or two four-continents wide,
They'll reach Buddhahood, with nowhere to hide.

Or from one four-continent, a dusty, single plea,
One more lifetime, Lord, then All-Knowing Wisdom they'll see.
All these beings, hearin' his long, long span,
Got pure karmic rewards, a divine master plan.

Dust motes of eight worlds, they hear his sweet decree,
His life span spoken, oh Lord, for all to see.
They raise their minds supreme, in a soulful, thankful way,
For the World-Honored One, brighten' every day.

He preaches Dharmas, immeasurable and grand,
Benefits vast, like empty space, across the land.
Heavenly maṇḍārava, and mahāmaṇḍārava too,
Rainin' down like blessings, from skies of darkest blue.

Śakras and Brahmās, like Ganges sands they throng,
From countless Buddha lands, joinin' the joyful song.
Sandalwood and agarwood, a sweet, sweet perfume,
Fallin' scattered everywhere, chasin' away the gloom.

Like birds from the sky, they come flyin' low,
To make their offerings, Lord, to all the Buddhas we know.
Heavenly drums in the air, a natural, soulful sound,
Tens of thousands of robes, whirlin' all around.

Jeweled incense burners, precious and so bright,
Burnin' priceless incense, fillin' the world with light.
Naturally spreadin' everywhere, a fragrant, holy haze,
Makin' offerings to World-Honored Ones, throughout their endless days.

Great Bodhisattva assemblies, banners in their hand,
Hundreds of millions of kinds, across the sacred land.
They rise to the Brahma heavens, in a majestic, holy line,
Before each and every Buddha, a truly blessed sign.

Jeweled banners and canopies, hangin' low and deep,
Millions of verses sung, while the heavens weep.
They sing and praise Tathagatas, with a bluesy, soulful moan,
Such things we ain't never seen, Lord, we're not alone.

Hearin' his life span, immeasurable and free,
Everyone's joyful, Lord, for all eternity.
The Buddha's fame rings out, in the ten directions wide,
Benefitin' all beings, with nowhere left to hide.
All are complete in good roots, a righteous, holy quest,
To attain the supreme mind, and finally find their rest.
At that time, the Buddha said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya: "Ajita, if there is a sentient being who hears that the Buddha's life span is so long and far-reaching, and is able to give rise to a single thought of belief and understanding, the merit they attain is without any limit. If a noble man or noble woman, for the sake of Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi, practices the five pāramitās for eighty million million nayuta kalpas—the pāramitā of giving, the pāramitā of precepts, the pāramitā of patience, the pāramitā of diligence, and the pāramitā of meditation, but not the pāramitā of wisdom—and you compare this merit to the previous merit, it is not even one hundredth, one thousandth, one hundred-millionth of it. It cannot be known by any calculation or parable. If a noble man or noble woman has such a merit, it is impossible for them to retrogress from Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Such people, Will have no doubt about this.
If a soul seeks the Buddha's light,
Through eighty million kalpas, day and night,
And five pāramitās, so strong and true,
Yeah, that's what a seeker's got to do.

Through those long kalpas, he gives and he prays,
To Buddhas and pratyekas, in a spiritual haze,
To Bodhisattva assemblies, a glorious sight,
Offerings made with all his might.

Rare food and precious, a feast for the soul,
Fine robes and bedding, making spirits whole,
Sandalwood halls for their peaceful rest,
Gardens around them, put to the test.

Such acts of giving, subtle and sweet,
Through all these kalpas, on weary feet,
To the Buddha Way, his heart he lays,
Yeah, through all those long and winding days.

If he upholds the precepts, pure and clean,
No flaws within him, a righteous scene,
And seeks the supreme Way, so high and grand,
All Buddhas will praise him, across the land.

If patience he practices, a gentle soul,
Harmony's ground, making his spirit whole,
Though evils surround him, a troublesome fray,
His mind won't be moved, come what may.

If arrogant ones, with Dharma's claim,
Torment and despise him, bringing him shame,
He'll endure it all, with a steady hand,
Yeah, that's the true spirit, across the land.

If he's diligent, his resolve stands firm,
For millions of kalpas, through every storm,
His mind is one, no laziness there,
Just steady devotion, beyond compare.

For countless kalpas, in a secluded spot,
Whether sitting or walking, whatever he's got,
Sleepiness banished, his mind he keeps clear,
Yeah, banishing doubt and every fear.

Because of this cause, meditations he'll find,
For eighty million kalpas, a peaceful mind,
No scattering thoughts, just a tranquil place,
Blessed with one mind, showing his grace.

With blessings of this mind, the Way he'll seek,
Till All-Knowing Wisdom, his spirit will speak,
Exhausting all states of meditation deep,
Yeah, those promises he's bound to keep.

This person, for hundreds of thousands of years,
Will cultivate merits, drying all tears,
As I have spoken, from way up above,
With wisdom and mercy, and endless love.

If a noble man or woman, hears my tale,
Of my life's long journey, they won't fail,
With faith for a single thought, pure and true,
Their blessings will surpass all that they do.

If a person has no doubt, no regret,
Believes with a deep heart, they won't forget,
Their blessings will flow, like a mighty stream,
Yeah, like a beautiful, sweet, soulful dream.

If Bodhisattvas, for kalpas untold,
Have practiced the Way, brave and bold,
When they hear my life span, so long and wide,
They'll believe and accept it, deep inside.

Such people, who receive this word from on high,
Vow for a long life, beneath the sky,
To save sentient beings, with hearts so pure,
Yeah, their compassion will surely endure.

Just like the World-Honored, today we see,
The King among Śākyas, wild and free,
Roaring a lion's roar, at the sacred place,
Preaching the Dharma, with fearless grace.

We in the future, revered we'll be,
When we sit where enlightenment sets us free,
We'll speak of our life span, the very same way,
Yeah, we'll sing our own blues, come what may.

If people with deep hearts, honest and bright,
Heard much and uphold it, with all their might,
Understand Buddha's words, in every true sense,
Yeah, they'll have no doubt, building their defense.
"Furthermore, Ajita, if there is a person who hears that the Buddha's life span is long and far-reaching and understands its meaning, the merit this person attains is without any limit. They are able to give rise to the Buddha's supreme wisdom. How much more so for one who widely hears this sutra, or causes others to hear it? Or if they uphold it themselves, or cause others to uphold it? Or if they copy it themselves, or cause others to copy it? Or if they make offerings to the sutra scrolls with flowers, incense, garlands, banners, canopies, fragrant oil, and ghee lamps? The merit of this person is immeasurable and boundless. He is able to give rise to All-Knowing Wisdom.
"Ajita, if a noble man or noble woman hears me speak of my long and far-reaching life span and believes and understands it with a deep heart, they will be as if they see the Buddha constantly on Mount Vulture Peak, surrounded by great Bodhisattvas and śrāvaka assemblies, preaching the Dharma. They will also see this Saha world with a ground of lapis lazuli, level and smooth, with golden ropes lining the eight roads. Jeweled trees will stand in rows, and all the towers and pavilions will be made of jewels. The Bodhisattva assemblies will all be dwelling within it. If a person can contemplate in this way, you should know that this is a sign of deep belief and understanding."
"Furthermore, after the Tathagata’s passing, if a person hears this sutra and does not slander it but gives rise to a joyful heart, you should know that this is also a sign of deep belief and understanding. How much more so for one who reads, recites, and upholds it? Such a person is bearing the Tathagata on his head.
"Ajita, this noble man or noble woman does not need to build a stupa or monastery for me or make the four kinds of offerings to the monastic community. Why? This noble man or noble woman who receives, upholds, reads, and recites this scripture has already built a stupa, erected monasteries, and made offerings to the monastic community. They have already built a seven-jeweled stupa with the Buddha’s relics, which is tall and vast, gradually becoming smaller as it reaches the Brahma heavens. They have hung banners and canopies and all kinds of jeweled bells from it, with flowers, incense, garlands, powdered incense, paste incense, burning incense, all kinds of drums, music, flutes, and harps. With wonderful voices, they sing and praise it. This is as if they have made these offerings for immeasurable millions of kalpas."
"Ajita, after my passing, if a person hears this scripture and is able to uphold it, or copies it himself, or causes others to copy it, he has already built monasteries. He has built thirty-two halls and pavilions with red sandalwood, which are eight tāla trees high, vast, and beautifully adorned. A hundred thousand monks will dwell within them. The gardens, bathing ponds, walking meditation areas, and meditation caves will all be complete with various wonderful things. Such monasteries and pavilions, numbering hundreds of thousands of millions, are immeasurable. With these, he makes offerings to me and the monastic community. For this reason, I say that after the Tathagata’s passing, if a person upholds, reads, recites, and preaches this sutra for others, or copies it himself, or causes others to copy it and make offerings to the sutra scrolls, he does not need to build a stupa or monastery and make offerings to the monastic community.
"How much more so for a person who can uphold this sutra and also practice giving, precepts, patience, diligence, a single mind, and wisdom? Their virtues are the most supreme, immeasurable, and boundless. Just as empty space—east, west, south, north, the four diagonals, above, and below—is immeasurable and boundless, the merit of this person is also immeasurable and boundless. He will quickly attain All-Knowing Wisdom. If a person reads and recites this sutra, upholds it, preaches it for others, copies it himself, or causes others to copy it, and is also able to erect a stupa, build monasteries, and make offerings to and praise the śrāvaka monastic community, and also uses hundreds of thousands of millions of methods to praise the merits of the Bodhisattvas, and for the sake of others, uses various causes and conditions to explain this Lotus Sutra according to its meaning, and is also able to purely uphold the precepts and live with those who are gentle, is patient and without anger, his resolve is firm, he always values sitting in meditation and attains various profound meditations, he is diligent and valiant, gathering all good Dharmas, and he has sharp wisdom and is skilled in answering difficult questions..."
"Ajita, after my passing, if a noble man or noble woman receives and upholds this scripture and has these wholesome merits, you should know that this person has already reached the place of enlightenment and is close to Anuttara-samyak-sambodhi, sitting under the bodhi tree. Ajita, if a noble man or noble woman is sitting, standing, or in a walking meditation area, a stupa should be erected right there. All the gods and humans should make offerings to it, as if it were the Buddha's stupa."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If after my passing, one can uphold this sutra,
The blessings of this soul, they're immeasurable, sure.
Just like I done told ya, said it plain and true,
The good that comes from this, gonna see you through.

This person, they done finished, all the offerings grand,
Built a stupa with relics, across the whole wide land.
Adorned with seven treasures, shining bright and bold,
A story of devotion, forever to be told.

Its jeweled shaft so tall, reaching for the sky,
Getting smaller as it climbs, way up where the Brahmins lie.
Precious bells in the millions, a sweet sound they make,
When the wind comes blowin', for goodness gracious sake.

For kalpas without number, they offer to this shrine,
With flowers and with incense, a love so divine.
Heavenly robes and music, a joyful, soulful praise,
And lamps of fragrant oil, burning through all days.

To constantly illuminate, all around the place,
In this evil age of Dharma, with its troubled pace.
One who can uphold this sutra, when the darkness calls,
Is like one who's done completed, all these offerings in the halls.

If one can uphold this sutra, it's as if the Buddha's near,
Dispelling all the shadows, calming every fear.
He makes offerings to a monastery, built with sandalwood so fine,
Thirty-two halls standing tall, eight tāla trees in a line.

With food so good and robes so grand, and bedding soft and deep,
A place for hundreds of thousands, secrets to keep.
With gardens bright and ponds for bathing, where the cool waters flow,
Walking meditation areas, where peaceful spirits grow.

Meditation caves so quiet, adorned in every way,
If a heart believes and understands, come what may.
Receives, upholds, or reads it, recites or makes a copy, too,
Or gets some others to copy, for me and for you.

And makes offerings to these scrolls, with flowers and incense sweet,
With powdered incense rising, beneath their holy feet.
With sumana and campaka, and atimukta flowers bright,
He keeps those lamps a-burning, through the long, dark night.

A person who makes these offerings, gets blessings without end,
Like empty space unending, a true and faithful friend.
His blessings are like that, immeasurable and wide,
How much more for one who holds this sutra, deep down inside.

And practices his giving, and keeps the precepts true,
With patience and with meditation, for me and for you.
Is not angry, speaks no evil, respects the stupas tall,
Is humble to the monks, standing up for one and all.

Far from arrogance he walks, contemplating wisdom's light,
If questions come a-calling, in the dark and weary night,
He's not angry, but compassionate, explains it clear and slow,
If one can practice this conduct, their merits truly grow.

If you see this Dharma teacher, with virtues so complete,
You should scatter heavenly flowers, right there at his feet.
And cover him with robes from heaven, a sight for weary eyes,
Bow your head before him, as if a Buddha from the skies.

You should think, "He's bound for glory, to enlightenment so true,
Attain the unconditioned, for me and for you.
And widely benefit the gods, and all of humankind,"
Such a spirit walking, a peaceful, loving mind.

His dwelling place, his walking, his sitting or his rest,
Or even where he preaches, a single verse, put to the test.
A stupa should be built there, adorned and wondrous to see,
And offerings received there, for all eternity.

A son of the Buddha dwelling, upon this sacred ground,
Is using Buddha's own tools, where truth can be found.
He's always in this place, through sunshine and through rain,
Walking, sitting, or lying, easing all the pain.



Chapter 18: Merits of Joyful Following
At that time, Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, if a noble man or noble woman hears this Lotus Sutra and rejoices in it, how much merit do they attain?" He spoke in verse, asking:
Since the Buddha done walked away,
Yeah, since the World-Honored One has passed on,
If a soul should chance to hear this Dharma today,
And find their heart rejoicin' 'fore the break of dawn,
Tell me, what kind of good fortune will that person claim?
What blessings fall upon 'em, speak the sacred name?
At that time, the Buddha said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Maitreya: "Ajita, after the Tathagata's passing, if a monk, a nun, a layman, or a laywoman, or any other wise person, whether old or young, hears this sutra, and having rejoiced in it, leaves the Dharma assembly and goes to another place—whether in a monastery, a secluded place, a city, a street, a village, or a field—and for their parents, relatives, good friends, or acquaintances, preaches what they heard according to their ability, and these people hear it and rejoice, and then in turn preach to others, and others hear it and also rejoice and preach in turn, and so on, until the fiftieth person in this chain. Ajita, I will now speak of the merit of the fiftieth person who rejoices. You should listen well."
The Merit of the Fiftieth Person
"Imagine in four hundred million million asaṃkhyeya worlds, there are all the sentient beings of the six destinies and four types of birth—those born from eggs, from wombs, from moisture, and from transformation; those with form and those without form; those with thought, those without thought, and those with neither thought nor no thought; and those with no feet, two feet, four feet, or many feet. In this great number of sentient beings, there is a person who seeks to plant blessings and gives them all the means of enjoyment they desire. He gives each and every one of these sentient beings enough gold, silver, lapis lazuli, tridacna, agate, coral, amber, and other wonderful precious treasures to fill the entire Jambudvīpa (the entire world). He also gives them elephants, horses, carriages, and jeweled palaces and pavilions. This great giver practices this charity for eighty years. He then thinks: 'I have given all sentient beings the means of enjoyment they wished for, but now these sentient beings are all old, over eighty years of age, with white hair and wrinkled faces, and will soon die. I should now instruct and guide them with the Buddha Dharma.' He then gathers all these sentient beings and proclaims the Dharma, revealing and teaching it to them, bringing them benefit and joy. They all, at once, attain the fruit of a srotāpanna, a sakṛdāgāmin, an anāgāmin, and an arhat. They end all their defilements, attain freedom in profound meditation, and are complete in the eight liberations.
What do you think, Maitreya? Is the merit gained by this great giver great?"
Maitreya said to the Buddha: "World-Honored One, the merit of this person is very great, immeasurable, and boundless. If this giver had only given all sentient beings the means of enjoyment, his merit would be immeasurable, let alone causing them to attain the fruit of an arhat."
The Buddha said to Maitreya: "I will now tell you clearly. The merit of that person who gave all the means of enjoyment to the sentient beings of the six destinies in four hundred million million asaṃkhyeya worlds and also caused them to attain the fruit of an arhat, is not even one-hundredth, one-thousandth, or one-hundred-millionth of the merit of the fiftieth person who hears a single verse of the Lotus Sutra and rejoices in it. It is beyond the reach of any calculation or parable. Ajita, the merit of this fiftieth person in the chain who rejoices in the Lotus Sutra is still immeasurable, boundless, and asaṃkhyeya, how much more so for the person who first heard it in the assembly and rejoiced? Their blessings are even greater, immeasurable, boundless, and asaṃkhyeya, and cannot be compared."
The Merit of Simple Acts
"Furthermore, Ajita, if a person, for the sake of this sutra, goes to a monastery and, whether sitting or standing, listens for a moment and accepts it, because of this merit, in their next life, they will be reborn with good and wonderful elephants, horses, carriages, jeweled palanquins, and will even ride in a heavenly palace. If a person is sitting in a place where the Dharma is being preached and another person comes and is encouraged to sit and listen, or if they give a part of their seat to them, the merit of this person is such that in their next life, they will be reborn in the seat of Śakra, or on the seat of the Brahma King, or on the seat of a wheel-turning holy king."
"Ajita, if a person says to another person: 'There is a sutra named the Lotus Sutra; we should go and listen to it together,' and that person accepts the instruction and goes and listens for even a moment, the merit of this person is such that in their next life, they will be reborn in the same place as a Bodhisattva with dhāranīs. They will have sharp faculties and wisdom, and for hundreds of thousands of lifetimes, they will never be mute. Their breath will never be foul, their tongue will always be free of sickness, and their mouth will also be free of sickness. Their teeth will not be dirty, black, or yellow; they will not be gapped, crooked, or missing. Their lips will not be drooping, retracted, coarse, or chapped. They will not be broken or slanted. They will not be thick or large, or dark and black. They will not have any unpleasant characteristics. Their lips, tongue, and teeth will all be beautifully adorned. Their nose will be long, high, and straight. Their face will be round and full. Their eyebrows will be high and long. Their forehead will be broad, level, and straight. They will be complete in the marks of a human being. In every life in which they are born, they will see the Buddha, hear the Dharma, and believe and accept his teachings."
"Ajita, you should consider this: the merit of encouraging a single person to go and listen to the Dharma is like this. How much more so for one who listens with a single mind, preaches and recites it, and for the great assembly, explains it for others and practices it as it is taught?"
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If a soul, at a sacred assembly, hear this holy word,
Yeah, even just one verse, that's all that's heard.
They feel that joy deep down, and they pass that word along,
From one to fifty folks, singin' the blessed song.
Now the last one, number fifty, the blessings that they gain,
I'm gonna lay it out now, gonna make it plain.

Imagine a man with a giving heart, a generous soul indeed,
For eighty long years he met every single need.
Countless folks he looked after, gave 'em all they desired,
But then he saw the twilight, the lamp that's been expired.
White hair and faces wrinkled, the signs of fading light,
Gaps in their teeth, bodies weary, near the endless night.
He said, "I gotta teach 'em now, gotta lead 'em to the Way,"
Used clever words and wisdom, on that very day.
Preached that true Dharma of peace, called nirvana's sweet release:
"This world ain't built to last, no, it's just foam and fleeting flame,
You gotta shake off these burdens, cry out freedom's name."
When those weary people heard that truth ringin' clear,
They all found the arhat's fruit, wiped away every tear.
They got those six magic powers, three wisdoms shinin' bright,
And eight sweet liberations, walkin' in the pure light.

But that fiftieth person, who just heard one sweet line,
Man, their blessings are bigger, they truly shine.
No way to compare it, no story I can tell,
They're higher than that great giver, free from the painful spell.
And the blessings of those who hear it, passed down from hand to hand,
They're still too vast to measure, across this weary land.
How much more for the first ones, right there in the sacred place,
Who heard it and rejoiced, showin' that joyful face?

If a body tells another, "Come on and lend an ear,
To the Lotus Sutra's wisdom, banish all your fear."
Sayin', "This book is deep and subtle, a treasure hard to find,
Through thousands of long ages, it weighs heavy on the mind."
And that person takes the lesson, and they go and take a seat,
Hearin' it just for a moment, oh, that sound so sweet.
The good karma they pile up, the reward they're gonna know,
I'm gonna break it down for you, watch that blessing grow.

No sickness in their mouth, Lord, never a troubled sound,
Teeth straight and white and perfect, never gapped or turned around.
No thick or shrunken lips, nothin' that looks amiss,
No ugly sight upon them, just beauty in their kiss.
Tongue ain't dry or black or short, but graceful and alive,
Nose is high and long and straight, a blessing to survive.
Forehead's broad and smooth and level, the picture of good grace,
And a majestic, lovely beauty shines right out of their face.
Folks will love to look upon them, their breath will be so clean,
Sweet as the utpala flower, the finest you have seen,
That fragrance comin' from their mouth, a wonder to behold.

If someone walks to the monastery, a story to be told,
Just to hear the Lotus Sutra, takes a step inside the door,
And for a moment they feel glad, they ask for nothing more.
I'm gonna talk about their blessings, the harvest they will reap,
In a life yet to come, Lord, while the world's asleep.
Among the gods or humans, they'll be reborn with style and might,
With chariots and fine horses, and jeweled things shining bright.
Ridin' in a heaven palace, a place of pure delight.

If in the preaching hall, one tells a soul to stay,
"Sit down and listen to the Sutra," and they point out the Way.
The blessings from that small kindness, what they're gonna gain,
A royal throne is waiting, Śakra or Brahmā's reign.
Or a wheel-turning king's high seat, mighty and serene.
How much more for the one who listens, with a heart sincere and true,
Explains what the words mean, and then they live it too?
Their blessings, they're beyond measure, like the stars above the blue.



Chapter 19: Merits of a Teacher of the Dharma
At that time, the Buddha said to Bodhisattva-Mahāsattva Constant Diligence: "If a noble man or noble woman receives and upholds this Lotus Sutra, or reads, recites, explains, or copies it, this person will attain eight hundred merits for the eye, twelve hundred merits for the ear, eight hundred merits for the nose, twelve hundred merits for the tongue, eight hundred merits for the body, and twelve hundred merits for the mind. With these merits, their six sense faculties will be adorned and made completely pure.
"This noble man or noble woman, with their pure physical eyes, born from their parents, will see all the mountains, forests, rivers, and seas, both inside and outside the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world. He will see down to the Avīci hell and up to the highest heaven. He will also see all the sentient beings within them, and their karmic causes, effects, and places of rebirth. He will see and know all of this."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If in the great hall, oh, a great assembly,
A fearless heart preaches the Sutra, you see.
You oughta listen close to the good he can do,
Eight hundred supreme eye merits gonna follow through.

With those bright blessings, his eyes get mighty pure,
Eyes born of his folks, oh, they're gonna endure.
They'll see the whole world, three thousand wide and deep,
From Mount Meru's insides to the secrets it keeps.

The Iron Ring Mountains, the forests and the trees,
The great big ocean, oh, down on his knees.
Down to the Avīci hell, way down below,
Up to the highest heaven, where the good winds blow.

All the souls in between, he'll see 'em all around,
Ain't got the heavenly eye, but the power abounds.
That physical eye power, it's somethin' like this,
Oh, the truth is laid open with a mighty fine kiss.

The Merits of the Ear
"Furthermore, Constant Diligence, if a noble man or noble woman upholds this sutra, or reads, recites, explains, or copies it, they will attain twelve hundred merits for the ear. With this pure ear, they will hear all kinds of languages and sounds, both inside and outside the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world, from the lowest Avīci hell to the highest heaven. The sounds of elephants, horses, cattle, and carriages; the sounds of crying and sighing; the sounds of conches, drums, bells, and chimes; the sounds of laughter and speech; the sounds of men, women, boys, and girls; the sounds of the Dharma and the sounds of non-Dharma; the sounds of suffering and the sounds of joy; the sounds of ordinary people and the sounds of sages; the sounds of happiness and the sounds of unhappiness; the sounds of gods, dragons, yakshas, gandharvas, asuras, garudas, kinnaras, and mahoragas; the sounds of fire, water, and wind; the sounds of the hells, the animal realm, and the hungry ghost realm; the sounds of monks and nuns; the sounds of śrāvakas and pratyekabuddhas; the sounds of Bodhisattvas and the sounds of the Buddhas. To put it simply, with their pure, ordinary ear born of their parents, they will hear and know all sounds, both inside and outside the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world. In this way, they will differentiate all kinds of sounds without damaging their ear faculty."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
The ear born of his parents
Is pure and without defilements.
With this ordinary ear, he hears
The sounds of the three-thousand-fold world.
The sounds of elephants, horses, carriages, and cattle,
The sounds of bells, chimes, conches, and drums,
The sounds of lutes, harps, and lyres,
And the sounds of flutes and pipes,
The wonderful sounds of pure songs—
He hears them all without being attached.
Countless kinds of human voices,
He hears and can understand them all.
He also hears the sounds of all the gods,
The subtle and wonderful sounds of their songs,
And he hears the sounds of men and women,
And the sounds of boys and girls.
In the deep and dangerous places of mountains and rivers,
The sounds of the kalaviṅka bird,
The jīvaṃjīvaka bird, and other birds,
He hears all their sounds.
The various sounds of suffering and agony
In the hells, he hears.
The hungry ghosts, pressed by hunger and thirst,
Their sounds of seeking food and drink, he hears.
All the asuras,
Who dwell by the great ocean,
When they speak to one another,
Their great sounds, he hears.
The one who preaches the Dharma is like this,
Dwelling in this place,
He hears these many sounds from afar,
Without damaging his ear faculty.
In the worlds of the ten directions,
The sounds of birds and beasts calling to each other,
The one who preaches the Dharma
Hears all of them from here.
In all the Brahma heavens,
The Abode of Light and the Abode of Purity,
And up to the highest heaven,
The sounds of their languages,
The Dharma teacher dwelling here,
Can hear all of them.
All the assemblies of monks
And all the nuns,
Whether they are reading and reciting the scriptures,
Or preaching to others,
The Dharma teacher dwelling here
Can hear all of them.
Furthermore, there are Bodhisattvas,
Who read and recite the scriptures,
Or preach to others,
Or write and explain their meaning.
All these sounds,
He can hear them all.
The great, holy, World-Honored Buddhas,
Who convert sentient beings,
In all the great assemblies,
Expound the subtle and wonderful Dharma.
The one who upholds this Lotus Sutra
Can hear all of them.
The sounds, both inside and outside,
Of the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world,
From the lowest Avīci hell,
To the highest heaven,
He hears all their sounds,
Without damaging his ear faculty.
Because his ear is so sharp and subtle,
He can differentiate and know them all.
The one who upholds this Lotus Sutra,
Although he has not yet attained the heavenly ear,
By using the ear born of his parents,
The merit is already like this.

The Merits of the Nose
"Furthermore, Constant Diligence, if a noble man or noble woman upholds this sutra, or reads, recites, explains, or copies it, they will attain eight hundred merits for the nose. With this pure nose, they will smell all kinds of fragrances, both inside and outside the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world, from above and below. The fragrances of sumana flowers, jāti flowers, mallikā flowers, campaka flowers, and parūṣaka flowers; the fragrances of red, blue, and white lotus flowers; the fragrances of flowering trees and fruit trees; the fragrances of sandalwood, agarwood, tāmalapattra incense, and tagara incense; and thousands of kinds of blended incenses, whether in powder, ball, or paste form.
"The one who upholds this sutra, dwelling in this place, can differentiate all of these. He can also differentiate the fragrances of sentient beings—the fragrance of elephants, horses, cattle, and sheep; the fragrance of men, women, boys, and girls; and the fragrance of shrubs and forests. All fragrances, whether near or far, he can hear them all and differentiate them without any error.
"The one who upholds this sutra, although dwelling here, can also smell the fragrances of the heavenly beings—the fragrance of the pārijāta and kovidāra trees, and the fragrances of maṇḍārava flowers, mahāmaṇḍārava flowers, mañjūṣaka flowers, and mahāmañjūṣaka flowers; and the fragrances of sandalwood, agarwood, and various powdered incenses and blended floral incenses. He can hear all of these fragrances. He also hears the body fragrance of the heavenly beings. He hears the fragrance of Śakra, the king of the gods, when he is in his wonderful palace enjoying the five desires. He hears the fragrance of when he is in the wonderful Dharma hall preaching the Dharma for the gods of the Thirty-three Heavens. He hears the fragrance of when he is playing in the gardens. He also hears the body fragrance of the other gods, men, and women, from a distance. In this way, he can hear the body fragrances of the heavenly beings, all the way up to the Brahma world and the highest heaven. He can also smell the incense that the heavenly beings burn, and the fragrances of the śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, Bodhisattvas, and all the Buddhas’ bodies, and he knows where they are. Although he hears all these fragrances, his nose faculty is not damaged or confused. If he wishes to differentiate them and preach them for others, he can remember them without error."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
Oh, that person's nose is pure, yes indeed,
It smells every kind of thing, that's what he can read.
Fragrant and foul, they all come through the air,
He knows the sweet perfume and the stink that's there.

Sumana, jāti, tāmalapattra so fine,
Sandalwood and agarwood, make your spirit shine.
Cassia and flowers, and the fruits from the vine,
He smells every living thing, he draws the line.

Man and woman's scent, he knows it where they be,
Dharma teacher far away, he knows by the decree.
Wheel-turning king and his sons, ministers too,
Palace women's perfume, he smells 'em comin' through.

Precious things they wear, and the treasure in the ground,
Precious queen of the king, he knows where she is found.
Robes and garlands they got on, perfumed oils they use,
He knows the very person, when he catches those blues.

Gods walkin' and sittin', playin' games on high,
Sutra holder knows it all, when that sweet scent drifts by.
Trees and flowers and fruit, the ghee and the oil,
Sutra holder right here, knows the fruit of the soil.

Deep in the mountains, dangerous and dark,
Sandalwood trees are bloomin', leavin' their mark.
Sentient beings inside, hidden from the eye,
He smells every single one, as the long years go by.

Iron Ring Mountains, and the oceans so wide,
Creatures in the earth, nowhere can they hide.
Sutra holder can smell 'em, know just where they are,
He's got that power of the nose, shinin' like a star.

Asuras fightin' and playin', both man and the wife,
He knows all their secrets, through the scent of their life.
Wilderness secluded, lions and wolves roam,
Tigers and elephants, far away from home.

Wild oxen, water buffalo, wanderin' around,
He knows where they're hidin', on the dusty ground.
Pregnant woman standin', he can tell you for sure,
If it's a boy or a girl, knockin' at the door.

Not men and non-humans, he knows their kind,
He smells all their presence, leaves nothin' behind.
Power of the smellin', he knows conception's start,
If the birth will be peaceful, a blessin' for the heart.

Good or not successful, he smells the whole long tale,
That powerful sense of smell, will never fail.
Thoughts of men and women, he knows 'em like a book,
Greed and desire, and the hateful, foolish look.

Cultivatin' goodness, he smells that sweet release,
He knows what's in their spirit, and brings 'em inner peace.
Hidden treasures underground, silver and the gold,
Copper vessels holdin' 'em, stories to be told.

He smells the jewels layin' there, knows the heavy cost,
Nothin' in the world of scents, is ever lost.
Garlands of all kinds, their value hard to say,
He knows if they're precious, come what may.

Precious or cheap, he knows their startin' line,
He knows their location, by a scent so fine.
Heavenly flowers bloomin', maṇḍārava sweet,
Mañjūṣaka and pārijāta, can't be beat.

He knows all those blossoms, by the scent they give,
He knows where the angels, and the holy spirits live.
Heavenly palaces, better and worse,
Jeweled flowers adornin' 'em, shakin' off the curse.

He smells all the mansions, up in the sky so vast,
From the first to the lowest, their power will last.
Heavenly gardens and halls, where the Dharma is told,
They enjoy their time there, precious as the gold.

He smells all the pleasure, and the peace they possess,
That powerful nose of his, can't confess nothin' less.
Gods listenin' to the Dharma, or enjoyin' the five desires,
Comin', goin', sittin' down, burnin' with those fires.

He knows all their actions, from the scent in the air,
He knows what they're up to, without a care.
Clothes of the maidens, flowers in their hair,
Travelin' and playin' games, without a care.

He knows all their beauty, and the places they roam,
That power of smellin', brings the truth back home.
Ascendin' up higher, to the Brahma world above,
He knows who's in meditation, with that peaceful love.

He smells 'em enterin' and leavin', knows the time and the place,
He sees the light around 'em, by the scent of their grace.
Abode of Light and Purity, up to the highest height,
He knows their birth and passin', in the dark and the light.

He smells their whole journey, from the cradle to the grave,
He's got that special power, he is strong and brave.
Monks in assembly, diligent and true,
Sittin' or walkin', knowin' what to do.

Readin' and recitin', concentrated mind,
Underneath the tall tree, the peace they find.
Sutra holder smells 'em, knows where they all dwell,
He's got that sacred power, that breaks the cruelest spell.

Bodhisattvas with resolve, steady as a rock,
Sittin' or readin', watchin' the tickin' clock.
Preachin' the Dharma to others, tellin' the holy word,
He smells all their good works, like a sweet singin' bird.

World-Honored Ones in every land, revered by all,
Preachin' out of pity, heedin' the world's sad call.
He knows all their teachin', and the wisdom they impart,
He smells the pure compassion, held within their heart.

Sentient beings listenin', hearin' the sacred song,
Joyful in the presence, where they truly belong.
Practicin' the Dharma, exactly as it's been taught,
He smells their true devotion, a battle bravely fought.

Hasn't got the Bodhisattva nose, of unconditioned truth,
But the holder of this sutra, has attained it in his youth.
He's got that first sweet smellin', a blessing to behold,
The power of the nose, worth more than finest gold.

The Merits of the Tongue
"Furthermore, Constant Diligence, if a noble man or noble woman upholds this sutra, or reads, recites, explains, or copies it, they will attain twelve hundred merits for the tongue. If they eat something that is good or bad, beautiful or not beautiful, or all things that are bitter and astringent, on their tongue faculty, they all become a supreme flavor, like the ambrosia of the heavens. Nothing is not delicious.
"If, with their tongue faculty, they expound anything in a great assembly, a profound and subtle voice will come forth that can enter the hearts of all and cause them to be joyful and happy. Furthermore, the heavenly gods and goddesses, and the gods of the Śakra and Brahma heavens, when they hear this profound and subtle voice, and his orderly explanation and words, they will all come to listen. And the dragons and dragon maidens, yakshas and yaksha maidens, gandharvas and gandharva maidens, asuras and asura maidens, garudas and garuda maidens, kinnaras and kinnara maidens, and mahoragas and mahoraga maidens, for the sake of hearing the Dharma, will all come to be close to him and make respectful offerings. And the monks and nuns, laymen and laywomen, kings, princes, ministers, and their families; the small and great wheel-turning kings, with their thousands of sons and all their relatives, will ride in their palaces and all come to listen to the Dharma, because this Bodhisattva preaches the Dharma so well. The Brahmins, elders, and the people of the land will serve and make offerings to him for their entire lives. Furthermore, the śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, Bodhisattvas, and all the Buddhas always delight in seeing him. In whatever direction this person is, all the Buddhas will preach the Dharma toward that place. He can receive and uphold all the Buddha Dharmas and can also emit a profound and subtle sound of Dharma."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
His tongue is pure, yeah, that much is true,
Never taste no evil, nothing bad to chew.
Every little morsel, every sip he takes,
Turns to sweet, sweet nectar, for goodness sakes.

He got a voice so deep, a sound so clear,
Preachin' the good Dharma for all to hear.
With stories and with wisdom, he shows the way,
Guiding troubled souls to a brighter day.

Folks hear his words, oh, they feel so fine,
Bringin' all their offerings, everything divine.
Gods and dragons listen, yeah, the whole wide crew,
Come with respect and listen to what he gotta do.

If this man wants his voice to rise and call,
Fillin' up the whole wide world, standin' up tall,
He just thinks it and it happens, don't you see,
A voice that shakes the heavens, wild and free.

Kings and their sons, a thousand strong and true,
Bow down with joined palms, see his message through.
Demons and night creatures, they get joy too,
Always comin' 'round to pay their dues.

Brahma and Mara, they can't stay away,
All them heavenly folks, comin' every day.
Even the Buddhas hear his blessed sound,
Keep him safe and steady on this earthly ground.

The Merits of the Body
"Furthermore, Constant Diligence, if a noble man or noble woman upholds this sutra, or reads, recites, explains, or copies it, they will attain eight hundred merits for the body. They will attain a pure body, like pure lapis lazuli, which sentient beings delight to see. Because their body is pure, all sentient beings of the three-thousand-great-thousand-fold world, from the time of their birth to the time of their death, whether they are superior or inferior, beautiful or ugly, or born in good or evil places, will all be reflected within it. And the Iron Ring Mountains, the Great Iron Ring Mountains, Mount Meru, Mount Mahāmeru, and all such mountains, and all the sentient beings within them, will all be reflected within it. Down to the Avīci hell and up to the highest heaven, all beings and all their surroundings will all be reflected within it. The Dharma preached by the śrāvakas, pratyekabuddhas, Bodhisattvas, and all the Buddhas will all be reflected as images within his body."
At that time, the World-Honored One, wishing to repeat this meaning, spoke in verse:
If a soul upholds the Lotus Sutra's call,
That body shines so pure, it stands up tall.
